10009{_}2058{_}He was not much in a race, and he decided to climb; so selecting a smooth-barked, perpendicular ash sapling, he started with might and main towards the top.
10009{_}2059{_}He went up, as he supposed, till he was out of the reach of the bear, and held on, all the time keeping his eye on the animal, and making as little noise as possible.
10009{_}2060{_}The bear, doubtless seeing that he was beyond her reach, passed on out of sight, and after he remained till the danger was over, he concluded to come down.
10009{_}2061{_}He was astonished to find that his efforts to descend were powerless.
10009{_}2062{_}He seemed to have frozen to the tree.
10009{_}2063{_}Upon looking around, to his utter amazement, he found himself sitting on the ground, _with both legs and arms locked fast around the, tree!
10009{_}2064{_}He had not climbed an inch, and the bear had not been aware of his presence in the woods!_  "That ash sapling was safe from that day.
10009{_}2065{_}It stood then in the old forest.
10009{_}2066{_}The woodman's axe spared it.
10009{_}2067{_}It stands now in the open field, a majestic tree; its great trunk, eight feet in circumference, its long arms covered with foliage, casting a broad shadow over the pasture beneath, in which cattle and sheep seek for coolness and ruminate in the heat of the summer days.
10009{_}2068{_}It is pointed out as the tree which the man who was frightened by a bear _didn't_ climb, and is referred to as evidence of the truth of my story, as the Dutchman proved the authenticity of his Bible, 'by the pictures.'"
10009{_}2069{_}"And that," said I, "puts _me_ in mind of a bear story, which has this merit over both of yours--it is true.
10009{_}2070{_}I can speak of it as a thing of personal knowledge, occurring within my own personal experience.
10009{_}2071{_}I began the study of law in Angelica, the county seat of Alleghany county, and as it was a good many years ago, it is fair to assume that I was a good many years younger than I am now, and that the country in that region was younger too.
10009{_}2072{_}Everybody knows that Alleghany county is, or used to be, a great place for whirlwinds and tornadoes.
10009{_}2073{_}If they do not, they may understand and be assured of the fact now.
10009{_}2074{_}A few years (less than twelve) ago, a black cloud came looming up in the northwest, and started on its career towards the southeast.
10009{_}2075{_}As it swept along, it sent its fierce winds crashing, and howling, and roaring, through the old forests, uprooting, hurling to the ground, and scattering everything that encountered its fury.
10009{_}2076{_}Houses, barns, haystacks, fences, trees, everything were prostrated, and to this day its track is visible in the swath it mowed through the old woods, from sixty to a hundred rods wide, plain and distinct still, for miles and miles.
10009{_}2077{_}It was not of that tornado, however, that I propose to speak.
10009{_}2078{_}Others had preceded it, and in the country all about Angelica were what were called 'windfalls.'
10009{_}2079{_}These windfalls were neither more nor less than the old tracks of these whirlwinds and tornadoes, that had swept down the forest trees.
10009{_}2080{_}Fire had finished what the whirlwind begun.
10009{_}2081{_}In time, blackberry-bushes had grown up among the charred trunks of the old pines, and other trees, bearing an immensity of fruit; and it was a pleasant resort for young people, one of those windfalls, when the blackberries were ripe and luscious.
10009{_}2082{_}These windfalls were great places, too, for rabbits, partridges, and 'such small deer,' and it was no great thing to boast of, to kill a dozen or two of the birds of an afternoon.
10009{_}2083{_}"I went out with a friend one day to one of these windfalls, partly after blackberries, and partly for partridges.
10009{_}2084{_}We were both boys, younger than fifteen, then, and each possessing, probably, quite as much discretion as valor.
10009{_}2085{_}We had separated a short distance from each other, he to gather berries, and I, with a small fowling-piece, in pursuit of game.
10009{_}2086{_}Presently I saw my friend crashing through the brush towards me, and also towards the fields, without his basket, and bare headed, his hair standing straight up, putting in his very best jumps, as if a thousand tigers were at his heels.
10009{_}2087{_}Without heeding for a moment my anxious inquiries as to what was the matter, he kept right on, leaping the logs like a deer, looking neither to the right hand nor the left, but with his coat tail sticking out on a dead level behind, making a straight wake for home.
10009{_}2088{_}Fear is said to be contagious, and I believe in the doctrine that it is so.
10009{_}2089{_}I caught it bad; and without knowing what I was afraid of, I started, and if any fourteen year old boy can make better time than I did on that occasion, I should like to see him run.
10009{_}2090{_}I kept possession of my fowling-piece, and came out neck and neck with my friend.
10009{_}2091{_}We scrambled over the outer fence, and ran some dozen rods or more in the open field, without either of us looking back.
10009{_}2092{_}Then, however, we made the astounding discovery, that there was nothing after us, and we both paused to take breath, and, so far as I was concerned, to ascertain, if possible, what had occasioned the race.
10009{_}2093{_}I learned that my friend, after I left him, had gone into the windfall, and was standing upon the long trunk of a fallen tree, picking berries, when he saw, a few rods from him towards the other end of the log on which he was standing, a great black hand reach up and bend down a tall blackberry-bush that was loaded with berries.
10009{_}2094{_}This alarmed him somewhat, for whoever the great black hand belonged to was concealed by the thick bushes and their foliage from his view.
10009{_}2095{_}Presently, two great black hands were placed upon the log, and a huge black bear clambered lazily up, and, for a second, stood in utter amazement, face to face, and within fifty feet of my friend.
10009{_}2096{_}Both broke at the same instant, in affright; my friend in one direction, and the bear in the other--my friend for the fields, and the bear for the deep woods--and each as anxious as fear could make him to put a 'broad belt of country' between them.
10009{_}2097{_}My friend dropped his basket, as he leaped from the log; it was no time to stop for a basket; a limb caught his hat and pulled it off; he had not time to stop for his hat.
10009{_}2098{_}The truth is, he was in a hurry, and something more than a hat or a basket was required to stay his progress towards home."
10009{_}2099{_}"The Squire's story," said Cullen, as he knocked the ashes from his pipe, and commenced shaving a fresh supply of tobacco with his jack-knife, and depositing it in the palm of his left hand, "the Squire's story reminds me of an adventer Crop and I met with, over towards St. Regis Lake, a good many year ago; and I'll state the circumstances of the case, as the Judge would say.
10009{_}2100{_}It was an adventer that don't happen often--leastwise, not in the same way.
10009{_}2101{_}It made me understand some things that I hadn't much idea of before.
10009{_}2102{_}Let me tell you, Judge, if you don't want a fight with an animal that's got long claws and sharp teeth, don't come close upon him onawares, or may be there'll be trouble.
10009{_}2103{_}Give him time to think, and ten to one he'll take to his heels.
10009{_}2104{_}Most animals have more confidence in their legs than they have in their teeth and claws, and they'll be very likely to use 'em, if you'll give 'em time to consider.
10009{_}2105{_}But if you find a painter, or a bear, takin' a nap in your path, and don't want to have a clinch with him, wake him up before you get right onto him, or he'll be very likely to think he's cornered, and them animals have onpleasant ways with 'em when they're in that fix.
10009{_}2106{_}"Wal, as I was sayin', Crop and I was over on St. Regis Lake, layin' in a store of jerked venison, and trappin' martin, and mink, and muskrat, and huntin' wolves, and sich other wild animals as came in our way.
10009{_}2107{_}The scalp of a wolf was good for fifteen dollars in them days, and a backload of furs was worth a heap of money.
10009{_}2108{_}We had a line of martin traps leadin' back to the hills, and over into a valley beyond, where the animal was plentier than they were on our side.
10009{_}2109{_}In passin' along this line, we had to round the end of a hill that terminated in a sharp point of rocks.
10009{_}2110{_}In a deep gully at its foot, a stream went surgin' over rapids; the bank on the side towards the hill was, may be, twenty feet high, and a right up and down ledge.
10009{_}2111{_}Above this ledge, and between it and the rocky point, was a narrow path, only three or four feet wide, that turned short around the end of the hill.
10009{_}2112{_}On the left hand was the ledge, and at the bottom of it were broken rocks, and on the right was a bluff point of rocks, that made up the end of the hill, standin' straight up, may be, fifty feet.
10009{_}2113{_}Around this point, the path turned sharp almost as your elbow.
10009{_}2114{_}"I was passin' quietly round this pint, lookin' down into the gully, with Crop at my heels, when, on turnin' the short elbow, there I stood, face to face, and within ten feet of a mighty big bear, that was travellin' my way, as the Judge said.
10009{_}2115{_}I had no idee that he was around, and I'm quite sartain he didn't expect to meet a human in such a place.
10009{_}2116{_}Of course, we were naterally astonished at seein' one another just then, and the meetin' didn't seem to be altogether agreeable to either party.
10009{_}2117{_}I ain't easily scared when I've time to prepare for a scrimmage, yet, I'm free to say, I'd have given a couple of wolf-scalps to've been on the other side of the gully, just at that time.
10009{_}2118{_}The bear seemed to expect me to begin the fight, for, after gruntin' out in a very oncivil way his surprise at makin' my acquaintance, he reared himself up on eend, and, with a fierce growl, showed a set of ivory that wasn't pleasant to look at.
10009{_}2119{_}I should have been willin' myself, to've backed down, and apologized for my rudeness in crossin' his path, for I was carryin' my rifle carelessly in my left hand, and our meetin' was so sudden that I scarcely had time to bring it to bear upon the kritter.
10009{_}2120{_}I rather think I should have dodged back, any how, but Crop seemed to think his master was in danger, and that he was obligated, live or die, to go in.
10009{_}2121{_}So, quick as a flash, he rushed by me, and threw himself into the very face of the desperate brute.
10009{_}2122{_}Crop made a great mistake when he calculated he was a match for that bear, for, with one cuff, the animal sent him eend over eend down the bank, upon the broken rocks below.
10009{_}2123{_}But the little time that was so occupied saved me a deal of trouble and danger, for it lasted just long enough for me to bring my rifle into position, which I did about the quickest, you may bet your life on that.
10009{_}2124{_}I run my eye along the barrel, sighted him between the eyes, and pulled.
10009{_}2125{_}The bear keeled over onto his back with a jerk, gave a spiteful kick with both hind feet, and he, too, went over the ledge onto the sharp rocks below.
10009{_}2126{_}I looked over, and saw Crop staggerin' to his feet, and lookin' about in a bewildered way, as if not quite understandin' how he came there.
10009{_}2127{_}I went round a little way, and got down into the gully where the animals were.
10009{_}2128{_}I found the bear stone dead, and Crop with two ribs broken and his shoulder out of joint, whinin', and moanin' piteously with pain.
10009{_}2129{_}I set his shoulder as well as I could, and, after takin' the skin off the bear, I backed him two miles to my shanty.
10009{_}2130{_}It was a fortnight before he 'left the house,' but he learned a little piece of wisdom by that cuff that sent him down the bank, and got a little insight into the nater of an angry bear."
10009{_}2131{_}[Illustration: Crop made a great mistake when he calculated he was a match for that bear, for, with one cuff, the animal sent him eend over eend down the bank, upon the broken rocks below.
10009{_}2132{_}But the little time that was so occupied saved me a deal of trouble and danger, for it lasted just long enough for me to bring my rifle into position, which I did about the quickest, you may bet your life on that.]
10009{_}2133{_}CHAPTER XX.
10009{_}2134{_}THE CHASE ON THE ISLAND--THE CHASE IN THE LAKE--THE BEAR--GAMBLING FOR GLORY--ANECDOTE OF NOAH AND THE GENTLEMAN WHO OFFERED TO OFFICIATE AS PILOT ON BOARD THE ARK.
10009{_}2135{_}We had as yet had no use for our dogs since we left the Saranac.
10009{_}2136{_}They had travelled quietly with us as we moved from place to place, or stayed inactive at the tents while we remained stationary.
10009{_}2137{_}The game was so abundant, that the real difficulty was to restrain ourselves from destroying more than was needful for our use.
10009{_}2138{_}We had indeed, failed to live strictly up to the law we had imposed upon ourselves, for we had at all times trout and venison beyond our present wants, excusing ourselves on the ground that an excess of supply was always preferable to a scant commissariat.
10009{_}2139{_}More than one deer was slaughtered, if the truth must be told, for no better reason than that given by an Irishman for smashing a bald head he chanced to see at a window: it presented a mark too tempting to be resisted the lake from our camping ground.
10009{_}2140{_}We stationed two of our boats between the island and the shore nearest the main land, and the other on the opposite side, and sent Cullen upon the island to beat for game.
10009{_}2141{_}It was scarcely five minutes, before the voices of the dogs broke upon the stillness of the morning, in a simultaneous and fierce cry, as if they had started the game suddenly, and fresh from his lair.
10009{_}2142{_}Away they went in full cry across the island, the deer sweeping around the upper end, and returning on the opposite side, as if loth to take to the water; but true to their instincts, the hounds followed, making the hills and the old woods ring again with the music of their voices.
10009{_}2143{_}Presently, a noble buck broke cover, directly opposite to where the Doctor and Smith's boat lay.
10009{_}2144{_}As our object was rather to enjoy the music of the chase, than to capture the deer, they shouted and hallooed as he entered the water, and he wheeled back, and went tearing in huge affright through the woods, up the island again.
10009{_}2145{_}Still the howling was upon his trail, and as he approached the upper end, he again took to the water, to be frightened back by Martin and myself, and with renewed energy he bounded across to a point stretching out into the lake on the opposite side.
10009{_}2146{_}Here Spalding and Wood were stationed, and they, by their shouting, drove him back again to the thickets.
10009{_}2147{_}By this time, the poor animal began to appreciate the full peril of his position, for turn where he would he found an enemy in front, while the cry of his pursuers followed him like his destiny.
10009{_}2148{_}Thus far every effort to escape by taking to the water had failed, and he seemed to think, as Martin expressed it, that "day was breaking."
10009{_}2149{_}He essayed it again on the land side, and was driven back by us, and thus he coursed three times round the island, until, in desperation, he plunged into the broad lake and struck boldly out for the opposite shore, three quarters of a mile distant.
10009{_}2150{_}Spalding shouted to us, and when we rounded the headland, we saw that he and Wood had headed, and were driving him towards a small island, of less than half an acre, covered only with low bushes, half a mile down the lake.
10009{_}2151{_}We did not propose to harm him, but we intended to drive him upon that little island, and by surrounding it, keep him there for a while by way of experimenting upon his fears, or rather as Martin said, "to see what he would do."
10009{_}2152{_}As he approached the shore, he bounded upon the island, and tossing his head from side to side, as if looking for a place of concealment or escape.
10009{_}2153{_}Finding none, he dashed across to the opposite side and plunged into the lake.
10009{_}2154{_}He was met by the Doctor and Smith, and turned back.
10009{_}2155{_}He rushed in another direction, across the island, to be headed by the boat in which I was seated, and again in another direction to be headed by Spalding.
10009{_}2156{_}Thus met and driven back at every turn, he at last stationed himself on a high knoll, near the centre of the island, apparently expecting that the last struggle for life was to be made there.
10009{_}2157{_}We rested upon our oars, making no noise, and watching his movements.
10009{_}2158{_}The bushes were low, coming only up midside to the animal.
10009{_}2159{_}He watched us latently for half an hour, tossing his head up and down, looking first at one, then at another, as if calculating from which the attack upon his life was to come.
10009{_}2160{_}At last, as if overcome by weariness, or concluding that after all there was no real danger, he laid quietly down.
10009{_}2161{_}In answer to his confidence in the harmlessness of our intentions, we rowed away back to the island where we started him.
10009{_}2162{_}We had not reached it, however, when we saw him enter the water, and swim to the main land, and glad enough he seemed to be when he had regained the protection of his native forests.
10009{_}2163{_}We took our dogs from the island, and rowed to the broad channel of the inlet which enters the lake on the left hand side, as you look to the south.
10009{_}2164{_}There are two of these inlets, which enter within a quarter of a mile of each other, each of which comes down from little lakes, or ponds, deeper in the wilderness.
10009{_}2165{_}The one we entered flows in a tortuous course through a natural meadow, stretching away on either hand forty or fifty rods, to a dense forest of spruce, maple, and beech, above which gigantic pines stand stately and tall in their pride.
10009{_}2166{_}Three miles from the lake, the hills approach each other, and the little river comes plunging down through a gorge, over shelving rocks, and around great boulders, as if mad with the obstructions piled up in its way.
10009{_}2167{_}As we approached these falls, Smith, who sat in the bow of the boat, motioned to the boatman to lay upon his oars, and pointed to an object partly concealed by some low bushes, forty or fifty rods in advance of us.
10009{_}2168{_}Remaining perfectly still a moment, we saw a bear step out upon a boulder, look up and down the stream, and stretch his long nose out over the water, as if looking for a good place to cross the rapids.
10009{_}2169{_}After scratching his ear with one of his hind feet, and his side with the other, he turned and walked deliberately from our sight into the forest.
10009{_}2170{_}By this time, the boat with the dogs came in sight, and we beckoned its occupants to come to us.
10009{_}2171{_}One of the hounds only had ever seen game of this kind.
10009{_}2172{_}But Cullen declared that there was no game that they would not follow when once fairly laid on.
10009{_}2173{_}We wanted that bear.
10009{_}2174{_}It was the only one we had seen; indeed it was the only one I had ever seen wild in the forest.
10009{_}2175{_}We went to the spot where we last saw him, and there in the sand, by the side of the boulder, was his great track, almost like a human foot.
10009{_}2176{_}Cullen called the attention of the dogs to it, and hallooed them on.
10009{_}2177{_}They took the scent cheerfully, and with a united and fierce cry they dashed away in pursuit.
10009{_}2178{_}They had ran but a short distance, when they seemed to become stationary, and deep, quick baying succeeded the lengthened and ringing sound of their voices.
10009{_}2179{_}"Treed, by Moses!"
10009{_}2180{_}cried Cullen, as he dashed forward, the rest of us following as fast as we could.
10009{_}2181{_}"Not too fast," said Martin, "not too fast.
10009{_}2182{_}There's no hurry; he won't come down unless our noise frightens him.
10009{_}2183{_}Let us go quietly; there's plenty of time.
10009{_}2184{_}Belcher has got his eye on him, and will stay by him till we come."
10009{_}2185{_}We travelled quietly, and as silently as we could for near half a mile, and as we rounded a low but steep point of a hill, there sat bruin, some twelve rods from us, in the forks of a great birch tree, forty feet from the ground, looking down in calm dignity upon the dogs that were baying and leaping up against the tree beneath him.
10009{_}2186{_}Did anybody ever notice what a meek, innocent look a bear has when in repose?
10009{_}2187{_}How hypocritically he leers upon everything about him, as if butter would not melt in his mouth?
10009{_}2188{_}Well, such was the look of that bear, as he peered out first on one side, then on the other of the great limbs between which he was sitting, secure, as he supposed, from danger.
10009{_}2189{_}But he was never more mistaken in his life.
10009{_}2190{_}In watching the dogs he had failed to discover us.
10009{_}2191{_}We agreed that three should fire upon him at once, reserving the fourth charge for whatever contingency might happen.
10009{_}2192{_}Smith, the Doctor, and Spalding sighted him carefully, each with his rifle resting against the side of a tree, and blazed away, their guns sounding almost together.
10009{_}2193{_}It was pitiful the scream of agony that bear sent up.
10009{_}2194{_}It was almost human in its anguish.
10009{_}2195{_}It went ringing through the woods, dying away at last almost in a human groan.
10009{_}2196{_}After struggling and clasping his arms for a moment around the great branch of the tree, his hold relaxed, he reeled from side to side, and then fell heavily to the ground, with three balls within an inch of each other, right through his vitals.
10009{_}2197{_}He was larger than a medium sized animal of his species, and in excellent case.
10009{_}2198{_}The next thing in order was to transport him to our boats.
10009{_}2199{_}This was done by tying his feet together, then running a long pole, cut for the purpose, between them, and lifting each end upon the shoulder of a boatman, he was "strung up," as Allen expressed it, clear from the ground.
10009{_}2200{_}They stumbled along as best they could, over the rough ground, and through the tangle brush, towards the river.
10009{_}2201{_}It was a heavy load considering the unevenness of the path, and the men were compelled to halt every few rods to breathe.
10009{_}2202{_}We got him safely to the landing at last, and tumbling him into the bottom of one of the boats, started down stream towards our shanty.
10009{_}2203{_}A proud trio were Spalding, Smith, and the Doctor that afternoon, returning with their game across the lake; and they certainly had some occasion to congratulate themselves, for this was the first wild, uncaged bear either of us had ever seen, and him they had succeeded in capturing.
10009{_}2204{_}We dined that afternoon on a roasted sirloin of bear, stewed jerked venison, fried trout, and pork.
10009{_}2205{_}I cannot say that I altogether relished the roast, though some of our company took to it hugely.
10009{_}2206{_}The truth is, that with some of them venison and trout were beginning to be somewhat stale dishes, they did not relish fat pork, and a change therefore to roasted bear meat was peculiarly acceptable.
10009{_}2207{_}"Gentlemen," said Smith to the Doctor and Spalding, as we sat after our meal, enjoying our pipes, "what say you to selling out your interest in that bear?
10009{_}2208{_}If you're open for a bargain, I'll make you a proposition."
10009{_}2209{_}"Why," the Doctor replied, "there'll be nothing left but the skin, and that will be of no special value except as a trophy."
10009{_}2210{_}"Not exactly," resumed Smith.
10009{_}2211{_}"I'll deal frankly with you, gentlemen.
10009{_}2212{_}There'll be a good many stories to be told about the killing of that bear, and my object is to appropriate the glory of the achievement.
10009{_}2213{_}Now it wont be a matter to boast of, to say that we three fired into one bear, and that none of the largest."
10009{_}2214{_}"Oh!
10009{_}2215{_}as to that," said the Doctor, "I intend to enlarge upon the subject, exaggerating the size of the bear, describing the terrible conflict I had with him, how I happened to save myself by remembering my double-barrelled pistol; how I made the three ball holes in him, while you and Spalding were running away, and how he bit me in the arm, and almost hugged me to death, while I was trying to get at the pistol.
10009{_}2216{_}I shall shine in that bear story!
10009{_}2217{_}Yes!
10009{_}2218{_}yes!
10009{_}2219{_}I shall shine!"
10009{_}2220{_}"Hear the cormorant!"
10009{_}2221{_}exclaimed Smith.
10009{_}2222{_}"Hear him!
10009{_}2223{_}And he'll do precisely as he says he will, only a great deal worse.
10009{_}2224{_}We must buy him out, Spalding.
10009{_}2225{_}We must purchase his silence for our own credit."
10009{_}2226{_}"Well, gentlemen," replied Spalding, "settle it between you--you are welcome to my share of the achievement.
10009{_}2227{_}The scream of mortal agony which that bear sent up when our three balls went crashing through its body rings in my ears yet.
10009{_}2228{_}I don't feel quite so proud of the shot as I otherwise should have done.
10009{_}2229{_}You are welcome to my share of the glory."
10009{_}2230{_}"Spoken like a liberal and free-hearted gentleman," said Smith.
10009{_}2231{_}"Well, Doctor, name the amount and nature of the blackmail you intend to levy upon me.
10009{_}2232{_}But have a conscience, man!
10009{_}2233{_}have a conscience!"
10009{_}2234{_}"It will be making a great sacrifice on my part," the Doctor replied, "but out of friendship for you, I'll make you a proposition.
10009{_}2235{_}We'll toss op a dollar, and the one that wins shall have the honour of having killed the bear, and of telling the story in his own way, and the others shall indorse it."
10009{_}2236{_}"Agreed," said Smith, "but if you win, I shall have to borrow a conscience of Spalding, or some other lawyer, for there'll be need of a pretty elastic one."
10009{_}2237{_}"Yours will answer, I think," drily remarked Spalding.
10009{_}2238{_}"It appears to me, gentlemen," said I "that I've something to say about the killing of that bear."
10009{_}2239{_}"You," exclaimed the Doctor, "what had you to do with it, pray?
10009{_}2240{_}There stands your rifle, with the same ball in it that you placed there this morning.
10009{_}2241{_}You haven't discharged your rifle to-day."
10009{_}2242{_}"Notwithstanding that," I replied, "I am entitled to a portion of the glory, as I am chargeable with my share of the responsibility, of killing the bear.
10009{_}2243{_}I was one of the first who discovered him; I was among the foremost in the pursuit; I was present, aiding and advising in the manner of the killing; I had my weapon in my hand, and was restrained from using it, only because you might fail to accomplish what my reserved bullet would have made secure.
10009{_}2244{_}Now, if this bear had been human, and we were accused of killing him, I would be regarded in the eye of the law as equally guilty with you.
10009{_}2245{_}I appeal to Spalding if this is not so?"
10009{_}2246{_}"H----is right," replied Spalding, as he sent a column of smoke wreathing upward from his lips.
10009{_}2247{_}"Such is the law."
10009{_}2248{_}"We must buy this fellow off, Smith," said the Doctor, "we must buy him off.
10009{_}2249{_}He's an old hunter, known as such, and he'll take to himself all the glory; and what is worse, the world will believe him.
10009{_}2250{_}He'll spread himself beyond all bounds.
10009{_}2251{_}He'll shine beyond endurance upon the strength of this bear.
10009{_}2252{_}We must buy him off.
10009{_}2253{_}It is against all conscience, but there is no help for it.
10009{_}2254{_}We must buy him off.
10009{_}2255{_}There's an impudence in this claim which reminds me of an anecdote related by Noah."
10009{_}2256{_}"By Noah?"
10009{_}2257{_}asked Smith, interrupting him, "Noah who?"
10009{_}2258{_}"What ignorance there is in this world, even in these days of educational enlightenment!"
10009{_}2259{_}remarked the Doctor to Spalding and myself.
10009{_}2260{_}"Now, here is a decently informed gentleman, claiming to be a Christian man, to have studied the Bible, and don't know who Noah was.
10009{_}2261{_}Such an instance of human ignorance in these times, is shocking."
10009{_}2262{_}"Oh!
10009{_}2263{_}I understand now," said Smith, "he was the gentleman who built the ark.
10009{_}2264{_}Well, go on with your anecdote."
10009{_}2265{_}"Well, as I was saying," the Doctor resumed, "this claim of H----'s to a share of the glory of slaying the bear, reminds me of an anecdote related by Noah soon after the subsidence of the flood, and it shows that impudence is, at least, not post-deluvian in its origin.
10009{_}2266{_}It seems that there were in the world before, as well as after the flood, some very meddling impudent fellows, who were always interfering with other people's business, claiming a share of other people's credit, trying to make the world believe that they were great things, and persuading everybody that whatever remarkable achievement was accomplished, occurred through their counsel and advice, and as a consequence, claiming a large share of all the honors going.
10009{_}2267{_}"Well, after the rain had continued falling for a number of days, and the valleys were all full of water, and the angry surges went roaring, with the voice of ten thousand thunders, high up along the sides of the hills, one of these pestilent fellows--deriding the miraculous exhibition going on all around him--undertook, in his self-conceit, to lead the people to a place of safety.
10009{_}2268{_}So he selected a lofty peak that shot up from a range of mountains, and commenced travelling up its steep acclivities.
10009{_}2269{_}But the flood followed him, roaring, and boiling, and heaving, in its onward rush.
10009{_}2270{_}Day by day, night by night, it crept up, and up, higher and higher, until the self-confident leader, who scoffed at the supernatural warning, had but a mighty small place above the surge, whereon to shelter himself from the destruction that surrounded him.
10009{_}2271{_}About that time the Ark, with Noah and his people, all safe and snug, came drifting that way.
10009{_}2272{_}"'Halloo!'
10009{_}2273{_}says the occupant of the rock, 'send us a boat, and take us aboard.
10009{_}2274{_}The freshet is getting pretty bad, and it is getting a little damp, up here.'
10009{_}2275{_}"'I can't do it,' says Noah, 'my craft is full of better people.'
10009{_}2276{_}"'But,' says the applicant for admission into the Ark, 'let me in, and I'll superintend the navigation.
10009{_}2277{_}I'll man the wheel, and see that the sails are all right, and we can pick up a deal of floating plunder as we go along.'
10009{_}2278{_}"'Can't do it,' says Noah, 'we've got a good steersman and safe navigators on board already.'
10009{_}2279{_}"'Well,' says the applicant, 'I'll work my passage as a deck hand, asking only a small portion of such spoils as we may pick up.
10009{_}2280{_}Come, bring us aboard.'
10009{_}2281{_}"'Can't do it,' says Noah, 'can't think of such a thing."
10009{_}2282{_}"'Then,' said the persevering applicant for a passage in the Ark, 'I'll go along for nothing--giving the benefit of my counsel and assistance free gratis; more than all that, I'll stand the liquor all round.'
10009{_}2283{_}"'No use in talking,' says Noah, 'you can't come on board of my craft, on any terms.
10009{_}2284{_}You'd corrupt my people, and set them by the ears in a week.
10009{_}2285{_}You can't have a berth on any conditions.
10009{_}2286{_}Good-bye!'
10009{_}2287{_}"'Then go to thunder with your old Ark,' indignantly responded the occupant of the rock, 'I don't believe there's going to be much of a shower, after all.'
10009{_}2288{_}"In a day or two, Noah drifted that way again.
10009{_}2289{_}The mountain peak had disappeared beneath the waters, and the occupants were all gone."
10009{_}2290{_}"I give up my claim," said I, "Doctor, in consideration of your anecdote.
10009{_}2291{_}Take the glory of killing the bear.
10009{_}2292{_}I see you're not disposed to give me a place in your Ark.
10009{_}2293{_}So toss up the dollar."
10009{_}2294{_}The dollar was tossed up, and Smith won the glory.
10009{_}2295{_}CHAPTER XXI.
10009{_}2296{_}THE DOCTOR AND HIS WIFE ON A FISHING EXCURSION--THE LAW OF THE CASE--STRONG-MINDED WOMEN.
10009{_}2297{_}The right to the glory of having killed the bear being settled, the Doctor, addressing himself to Spalding, remarked--"There was something in H----'s appeal to you about the law of his case, that reminded me of a little scene between my wife and myself, many years ago, when we were both younger than we are now, and certainly had never anticipated the dark years of trial, through which we were unexpectedly called upon to pass.
10009{_}2298{_}You know that I started in life, like Smith here, a gentleman of fortune, calculating, like him, to live at my ease, without troubling myself with the cares of any particular business, as I passed along.
10009{_}2299{_}Still I thought, or rather my father thought, that it would be well enough, even for a gentleman, to have at least a nominal title to some profession.
10009{_}2300{_}So I studied the law, and was admitted as an attorney and counsellor of the courts.
10009{_}2301{_}Never intending to practise, I did not become very profoundly learned in the profession; still I became, to some extent, indoctrinated with its mysteries.
10009{_}2302{_}I did not like it; and when the necessity for some active employment came looming up in the distance, I chose a different calling, and at six-and-twenty, commenced the study of my present profession.
10009{_}2303{_}This did not occur until after I had been married some three years.
10009{_}2304{_}I lived in the country then, or rather, summered there, in a beautiful little village in the interior of the State, in a pleasant, old-fashioned house, which my father built, and which, as I was his only heir, I supposed of course I owned.
10009{_}2305{_}Some half a dozen miles from the village was a fine trout stream, to which my wife and myself used occasionally to go on a fishing excursion.
10009{_}2306{_}On such occasions we went on horseback, as the road was somewhat rough, and my wife was as much at home in the saddle as I was.
10009{_}2307{_}This, I repeat, was a good while ago, and we were both a score of years younger than we are now.
10009{_}2308{_}Well, I started out alone one day to visit this trout stream, anticipating a good time with its speckled, and usually greedy inhabitants.
10009{_}2309{_}I say I was alone, and yet there was with me, all the way, and all the time, one who can talk, reason, philosophise, understand things as well as you or I; and one, to all appearance, as much and distinctly human as you or I."
10009{_}2310{_}"Impossible!"
10009{_}2311{_}exclaimed Smith, "we can't go that, Doctor.
10009{_}2312{_}I can't stand my quarter of that."
10009{_}2313{_}"Foolish man!"
10009{_}2314{_}continued the Doctor; "I say I was alone; let me demonstrate my proposition.
10009{_}2315{_}Blackstone says, and what he says every lawyer will concede is the end of the law, and the beginning too, for that matter, that when a woman becomes a wife, she loses her identity, becomes nobody; that her husband absorbs her existence, as it were, as he does her goods and chattels, in his own.
10009{_}2316{_}Now, sir, do you comprehend?
10009{_}2317{_}My wife was with me, and she, being according to law nobody, of course I was alone.
10009{_}2318{_}You, sir, being a law abiding man, must admit that my proposition is Q.E.D.
10009{_}2319{_}"The doctrine of absorption, as I call it, is convenient.
10009{_}2320{_}It promotes harmony of action, by subjecting it to the control of a single will, thus avoiding all embarrassment from a conflict of opinion between man and wife.
10009{_}2321{_}So, on my way to the trout stream (I say _my_ way, for though my wife was on horseback by my side, yet she being, according to the best legal authorities, nobody, you see I was alone), I thought I would enlighten the good lady in regard to the true position, or rather the no position at all, which she occupied.
10009{_}2322{_}Our way lay for a couple of miles along an old road, towards a clearing which had been abandoned, and through which the stream flowed.
10009{_}2323{_}The tall old trees spread their long arms over us, clothed in the rich verdure of spring, and the breeze, so fresh and fragrant, moaned, and sighed, and whispered among the leaves.
10009{_}2324{_}"'My dear,' said I, blandly, as we rode along, the birds singing merrily among the branches above us, 'do you know that you are NOBODY?'
10009{_}2325{_}"'Nobody, Mr. W----,' (I was simply Mr. W----then; I had not become, nor even dreamed that I should become a Doctor), 'Nobody, Mr. W----?
10009{_}2326{_}Did you say nobody?'
10009{_}2327{_}"'Absolutely nobody,' said I.
10009{_}2328{_}'A perfect nonentity.
10009{_}2329{_}You are less even than a legal fiction.'
10009{_}2330{_}"'Look you,' said she, as she applied the whip to her pony, in a way that brought him, with a bound, across the road directly in front of me (she rode like a belted knight), obstructing my progress, 'Look you, Mr. W----,' and there was a red spot on her cheek, and her eye sparkled like the sheen of a diamond, 'let us settle this matter now.
10009{_}2331{_}I can bear being of small consideration, occupying very little space in the world, but to be stricken out of existence entirely, to possess no legal identity, to be regarded as absolutely nobody, is a thing I don't intend to stand--mark that, Mr.
10009{_}2332{_}W----.'
10009{_}2333{_}"'Keep cool, my dear,' said I; 'let us argue this matter.'
10009{_}2334{_}I was calm, for I knew the law was on my side; I had the books, and the courts, and the statutes all in my favor.
10009{_}2335{_}I was fortified, you see.
10009{_}2336{_}"'Argue the matter!'
10009{_}2337{_}she exclaimed; 'not till it is admitted that I'm somebody.
10009{_}2338{_}If I'm nobody, I can't be argued with, I can't reason, nor talk.
10009{_}2339{_}Now, Mr. W----, I've a tongue.'
10009{_}2340{_}"'Gospel truth,' said I, 'whatever the authorities may say.
10009{_}2341{_}But we will admit, for the sake of the argument, that you are somebody; Blackstone says'----  "'Out on Blackstone,' she exclaimed; 'what do I care for Blackstone, whose bones have been mouldering in the grave for more than a hundred years, for what I know.
10009{_}2342{_}Don't talk to me about Blackstone.'
10009{_}2343{_}"'But, my dear, you are _my_ wife, and Blackstone says'--  "'I don't care a fig what Blackstone says.
10009{_}2344{_}If I _am_ your wife, I am my mother's daughter, and my brother's sister, and Tommy's mother, and there are four distinct individualities all centered in myself.'
10009{_}2345{_}"'But,' said I again, 'Blackstone says'--  "'Confound that Blackstone,' she exclaimed; 'I do believe he has driven the wits out of the man's head.
10009{_}2346{_}Now, look you, Mr. W----, you invited me to ride with you; you now say I am nobody.
10009{_}2347{_}Very well.
10009{_}2348{_}If nobody leaves you, I suppose you won't be without company, for somebody certainly left home with you this morning, and has rode with you thus far.
10009{_}2349{_}So, good-bye, Mr. W----; success to your fishing, Mr. W----,' and she struck into a gallop towards home.
10009{_}2350{_}"'Hallo!'
10009{_}2351{_}said I, 'I give up the point.
10009{_}2352{_}I take back all I said.
10009{_}2353{_}_Culpa mea_, my good wife.
10009{_}2354{_}If Blackstone does say'--  "'Not a word more about Blackstone,' said she, shaking her whip, half serious half playfully, at me; 'if I go with you, I go as somebody--a legal entity.'
10009{_}2355{_}"'Very well,' said I, 'we'll drop the argument.'
10009{_}2356{_}"'Not the argument, but the fact, Mr. W----; and admit that Blackstone was a goose, and that his law, like his logic, is all nonsense when measured by the standard of common sense and practical fact.
10009{_}2357{_}Admit that a woman, when she becomes a wife does not become a mere nonentity, or I leave you to journey alone.'
10009{_}2358{_}"'Very well, my dear, let us see if we cannot compromise this matter.
10009{_}2359{_}Suppose we allow his philosophy to stand as a general truth, making you an exception.
10009{_}2360{_}We'll say that wives in general are nobody, but that you shall be exempt from the general rule, and be considered always hereafter, and as between ourselves, as somebody.'
10009{_}2361{_}"You see the shrewdness of my proposition.
10009{_}2362{_}Firstly, it saved Blackstone; secondly, it saved _me_, let me down easy; and thirdly, it appealed to the womanly vanity of my wife, and it took.
10009{_}2363{_}"'Oh, well,' she said, as she brought her pony alongside of me, and we jogged along cosily together, 'I see no objection to that.
10009{_}2364{_}Other wives can take care of themselves.
10009{_}2365{_}But this compromise, as between _us_, Mr. W----, must be a _finality_.
10009{_}2366{_}No Nebraska traps, Mr. W----.
10009{_}2367{_}No Kansas bills hereafter.
10009{_}2368{_}It must be a finality, mind.'
10009{_}2369{_}"'Very well,' said I; and a robin that was building its nest on a limb that hung over the road, paused in its labors, and burst into song, and the burden of its lay seemed to be a compromise, which, in truth, should be a FINALITY.
10009{_}2370{_}"We were successful in our fishing, and we followed the old-fashioned custom as to bait.
10009{_}2371{_}We discarded the fly, using only the angle-worm.
10009{_}2372{_}At the foot of the ripples; under the old logs; where the water went whirling under the cavernous banks; in the eddies; among the driftwood; everywhere, we found trout--not large, none weighing over six ounces, and few less than three.
10009{_}2373{_}We caught my basket full in less then two hours, and then rode home.
10009{_}2374{_}It was a day of enjoyment to us, you may be sure.
10009{_}2375{_}"And now I appeal to you, in all seriousness, my friend," the Doctor continued, addressing himself to Spalding, "if there is not something due to the wives and mothers of the present generation?
10009{_}2376{_}Is there not some relaxation of the law necessary in vindication of the civilization of the age, against the legal barbarisms still remaining on the statute books, and adhered to by the common law, in regard to wives and mothers?
10009{_}2377{_}Is the current of progress to flow by them for ever, bearing no reforms which shall affect them?
10009{_}2378{_}Do not misunderstand me.
10009{_}2379{_}I am no advocate of the practices of the 'strong-minded women,' who hold their conventions and public meetings, who unsex themselves by mounting the forum, and, throwing off the retiring modesty of the true woman, seek to secure notoriety at the price of popular contempt.
10009{_}2380{_}But there are evils which bear heavily, too heavily, upon the women even of this country, and which, for the credit of the civilization of the age, should be corrected.
10009{_}2381{_}As calm-minded, philanthropic men, we, the American people, should look into this subject, and, regardless of jeer and scoff, do what justice, humanity, and the right demand of us, in regard to some of the social and legal inequalities between the sexes, pertaining to the married state."
10009{_}2382{_}"It is one of the mysteries of our system of jurisprudence," replied Spalding, "that while everything else is on the move, while progress is written in letters of living light upon all other things, that remains stationary--at least in a comparative sense.
10009{_}2383{_}The world moves on, civilization advances, science and the arts stride forward, but the law stands still.
10009{_}2384{_}A principle which may have been somewhat changed, modified, bent, if you please, into an adaptation to the exigencies of the present, and a fitness for the changed circumstances of the times in which we live, is suddenly thrown back into its old position by the exhumation of some 'decision' from the dust of ages, made by some judge away back in the olden times, resurrected by the research of some antiquarian lawyer, who loves to delve among the rubbish of past generations.
10009{_}2385{_}The learning, the wisdom, the philosophy of the present is discarded, and the spirits of a lower civilization are conjured from the darkness of vanished centuries, to settle rules for the government of commerce, personal conduct, and the social relations of the times in which we live.
10009{_}2386{_}There seems to be something paradoxical in the idea that the older the decision the better the law--the more ancient the commentator, the profounder the wisdom of his axioms.
10009{_}2387{_}This might be well, were it true that civilization is 'progressing backwards,' the science of government retrograding.
10009{_}2388{_}In that case, it would of course be true, that the nearer you approach the fountain, the purer the stream would be.
10009{_}2389{_}But such is not the fact.
10009{_}2390{_}In all these attributes the world is on the advance, the science of government progressive; and to make the wisdom of centuries ago override the wisdom, or overshadow the light of the present, is a paradox peculiar to our system of jurisprudence.
10009{_}2391{_}There are lawyers and judges, who enjoy a high reputation, whose fame rests upon their profound research among the worm-eaten tomes of black-letter law, and whose glory consists in their familiarity with the opinions and axioms of men who lived and died so long ago that their very tombs are forgotten.
10009{_}2392{_}This class of lawyers and jurists hold in contempt all the learning, the philosophy, the practical wisdom of the present --rejecting everything that is not bearded and hoary with age.
10009{_}2393{_}Seated in their libraries, in the midst of their ponderous octavos, their Roman and black-letter volumes, they reject with disdain the commentators, the opinions of the jurists of the present century; and brushing away the cobwebs and dust from the covers of their treasured relics of bygone ages, they clasp them in a loving embrace close to their hearts, exclaiming, 'These are my jewels.'
10009{_}2394{_}Whatever has not the sanction of ancient authority, is folly to them--worse than folly, for it is innovation, and that is rank impiety.
10009{_}2395{_}"I remember an anecdote of the celebrated William Wirt, related to show how ready his mind was, how instant in activity, and how suddenly it would flash with an eloquence, superior to that exhibited by the most elaborate preparation.
10009{_}2396{_}He was arguing a cause before the Supreme Court of the United States, and laid down, as the basis of his argument, a principle to which he desired to call the particular attention of the judges.
10009{_}2397{_}The opposing counsel interrupted him, calling for the authority sustaining his principle,--'The book--the book!'
10009{_}2398{_}demanded his adversary.
10009{_}2399{_}'Sir, and your honors,' said Wirt, straightening himself up to his full height, 'I am not bound to grope my way among the ruins of antiquity, to stumble over obsolete statutes, or delve in black letter law, in search of a principle written in living letters upon the heart of every man.'
10009{_}2400{_}If the idea contained in this answer of Wirt, were more fully appreciated by our modern jurists, it would be all the better for the country.
10009{_}2401{_}"The common law is said to be the perfection of reason.
10009{_}2402{_}This is doubtless true, but it is the perfection of the reason of the present, as well as of the past.
10009{_}2403{_}Its principles are elastic, suiting themselves to the civilization of all ages.
10009{_}2404{_}They are progressive, keeping pace with the progress of all times.
10009{_}2405{_}They are not immutable, save in the element of right, and they therefore shape themselves to all circumstances, moving along with the onward march of trade, the commerce, the social relations, and business of the people.
10009{_}2406{_}The learning of to-day, the wisdom, the philosophy of to-day is profounder than that of any preceding century, and it is folly to overthrow it by, or compel it to give place to, the learning, the wisdom, the philosophy of departed and ruder ages.
10009{_}2407{_}"In regard to your question, whether there is not some relaxation of the law necessary, in vindication of the civilization of the age, against the legal barbarisms remaining upon the statute book, and in the common law in regard to our wives, I answer frankly that I do not know about that.
10009{_}2408{_}The law, as you read it in Blackstone, and as you expounded it to your wife, on your fishing excursion, has been somewhat modified.
10009{_}2409{_}Wives have been given a _status_ by modern legislation; and a woman, by becoming a wife, does not now cease to be a legal entity.
10009{_}2410{_}The law permits her to retain and control her property irrespective of her husband, and she has, therefore, thus far, ceased to be 'nobody.'
10009{_}2411{_}But my private opinion is, that, as a general thing, the women of this country get along very well, even under the pressure of the 'barbarisms' of which you speak.
10009{_}2412{_}They manage, one way and another, to get the upper hand of their legal lords, law or no law.
10009{_}2413{_}If their existence, in the light of authority, is 'less than a legal fiction,' they come to be regarded, or make themselves felt in the world as practical facts.
10009{_}2414{_}They are quite as apt to be at the top, as at the bottom of the ladder, notwithstanding what 'Blackstone says' about their legal position.
10009{_}2415{_}There is, doubtless, a good deal of abuse of authority on the part of husbands, but the women get their share of the good that is going in the world, as a general thing.
10009{_}2416{_}If the law is against them, they manage to usurp full an even amount of privilege and authority, and keep along about in line with the other sex.
10009{_}2417{_}I never knew an out and out controversy between a man and his wife, in which the former did not get the worst of it in the end; and as to the impositions, which as a melancholy truth are too frequent, they are about as much on one side as the other.
10009{_}2418{_}It is not to legal enactments that we must look for the cure of unhappiness incident to the married state, but to a reform in temper and habits of life.
10009{_}2419{_}Besides, I do not believe the wives of this country would accept of a strict legal equality at all, if it were tendered them as a FINALITY.
10009{_}2420{_}I believe they would prefer remaining as they are; for by being so, they are left to the resources of their own genius, to win by their tact, what is not guaranteed by law.
10009{_}2421{_}I know that there are a good many crazy-headed people in pantaloons as well as petticoats, who go about laboring for the 'emancipation of women,' as if the heavens and earth were coming together.
10009{_}2422{_}But those of them who wear skirts, generally have delicate white hands, flowing curls, flashing black eyes, and the gift of oratory--and a desire to exhibit them all; while those in pantaloons have their hair combed smoothly back, as if preparing to be swallowed by a boa-constrictor, wear white cravats, talk softly, and show a good deal of the whites of their eyes, from a chronic habit of looking up towards the moon and stars.
10009{_}2423{_}As a general thing, these latter are of no practical use in the world, and make as good a tail to the kite of the 'strong-minded women' as anything else.
10009{_}2424{_}But these people represent a very small portion of the American women, and until the masses demand 'emancipation,' I rather think that matters had better be permitted to remain as they are.
10009{_}2425{_}The women will take care of themselves--no fear of that."
10009{_}2426{_}CHAPTER XXII.
10009{_}2427{_}A BEAUTIFUL FLOWER--A NEW LAKE--A MOOSE--HIS CAPTURE--A SUMPTUOUS DINNER.
10009{_}2428{_}We started the next morning on an exploring voyage up the right-hand stream, which enters this beautiful lake some half a mile west of the one we had looked into the day before.
10009{_}2429{_}On either hand, as we passed along the narrow channel, was a natural meadow, covered with a luxuriant growth of rank grass and weeds, conspicuous among which was a beautiful flower, the like of which I have never seen anywhere else.
10009{_}2430{_}I am no botanist, and therefore cannot describe it in the language of the florist, so that the learned in that beautiful science might classify it.
10009{_}2431{_}It resembles somewhat the wild lily in shape, growing upon a tall, strong stem, almost like the stem of the flag.
10009{_}2432{_}The flower itself is double, and its deep crimson--the deepest almost of any flower I have ever seen--shone conspicuously, as it waved gracefully in the breeze above the surrounding vegetation.
10009{_}2433{_}It has one defect, however; it is without fragrance, I infer from the fact that its roots spread far out every way, and reach down into the water beneath, that it can hardly be transferred to the garden, or become civilized.
10009{_}2434{_}It would be a great acquisition to the collection of the florist if it could, for I know of no flower that excels it in richness of color, gracefulness of appearance, or in gorgeousness of beauty.
10009{_}2435{_}We saw abundance of deer feeding quietly upon the narrow meadows, and upon the lily pads on our way.
10009{_}2436{_}We had no inclination to injure them, and we let them feed on.
10009{_}2437{_}Some of them were hugely astonished, however, at our presence, and dashed away, whistling and snorting, into the forest.
10009{_}2438{_}Two miles from the lake, we came to a rocky barrier, down which the stream, came rushing and roaring, for fifty or sixty rods, in a descent of perhaps sixty feet in all.
10009{_}2439{_}Around these rapids the boats were carried, and we found, above them, the water deep and sluggish, flowing through a dense forest, the tall trees on the banks stretching their leafy arms across the narrow channel, forming above it an arch delightfully cool, through which the sunlight could scarcely penetrate.
10009{_}2440{_}We followed this channel a long way, when we came to a little lake or pond, four or five miles in circumference.
10009{_}2441{_}It was a perfect gem, laying there all alone, so calm, so lovely in its solitude, with no sign of civilization around it, no sound of civilization startling its echoes from their sleep of ages, no human voice having perhaps ever been heard upon its shore since the red man departed from the hunting-ground of his fathers.
10009{_}2442{_}The shores all around it were bold and rocky, save on the western side, where a broad sandy beach, of a quarter of a mile in extent, lay between the water and the shadow of the deep forest beyond.
10009{_}2443{_}A solitary island of half a dozen acres, covered with majestic pines and tall, straight spruce trees, rises near the centre of the lake, adding a new charm to its quiet beauty.
10009{_}2444{_}The waters of this little lake are clearer and more transparent than those of any other we had seen; we could see the white shells on its sandy bottom, fifteen feet below the surface.
10009{_}2445{_}This peculiarity induced us to believe that we were above the stratum of iron ore which seems to underlay most of this wild region, coloring, while it does not render impure, the waters of most of these lakes and rivers.
10009{_}2446{_}I have frequently, in my wanderings in these northern wilds, stumbled upon outcropping orebeds, which, were they nearer market, or more accessible to the energy and enterprise of the American people, would be capable of building up gigantic fortunes, but they are all valueless here, and probably will continue so for generations to come.
10009{_}2447{_}We saw the fresh tracks of a moose on the sandy beach, tracks that had been made that morning, and we concluded to spend the day here, in the hope of securing one of these gigantic deer.
10009{_}2448{_}We rowed to the island, intending to encamp there.
10009{_}2449{_}We entered a little bay, of half an acre, the points forming it coming within a few yards of each other, and the branches of the trees intertwining their long arms lovingly above.
10009{_}2450{_}As we landed, our dogs began nosing and dashing about, as if suddenly roused into excitement by the hot scent of some animal that had been disturbed by our coming.
10009{_}2451{_}They broke into a simultaneous cry, and plunged like mad into the thicket.
10009{_}2452{_}We pushed our boat back towards the open water, when we heard the plunge of some animal into the lake, on the other side of the island.
10009{_}2453{_}Martin, who was in the leading boat with me, by a few vigorous pulls at the oar, rounded the point between us and the spot where we had heard the plunge, and there, not ten rods from the shore, making for the mainland, was the game which, of all others, we most desired to see.
10009{_}2454{_}"A moose!
10009{_}2455{_}by Moses!"
10009{_}2456{_}exclaimed Martin, in huge excitement.
10009{_}2457{_}"Hurrah!
10009{_}2458{_}hurrah!
10009{_}2459{_}A moose!
10009{_}2460{_}he's ours!
10009{_}2461{_}he can't escape!"
10009{_}2462{_}and away he dashed in pursuit.
10009{_}2463{_}The other boats now hove in sight, and a loud hurrah!
10009{_}2464{_}went up from each, when they saw the nature of the game that had been started.
10009{_}2465{_}There was no difficulty in overtaking the animal, desperate as were his efforts to escape.
10009{_}2466{_}We shot past him, and turned him back in a direction towards the island again, and I picked up my rifle to settle the matter.
10009{_}2467{_}"Don't shoot him," said Martin; "don't shoot him yet; he can't get away, and if you kill him, he'll sink; and if he don't, we can't get him into the boat.
10009{_}2468{_}Let us drive him back to the island."
10009{_}2469{_}The other boats were, by this time, up with us, every man in a wild state of excitement, eager to be first in at the death.
10009{_}2470{_}We had headed the animal towards the island, with our three boats so arranged, as that he could swim in no other direction, without running one of them down.
10009{_}2471{_}The dogs had started a deer that had taken to the water, on the other side of the island.
10009{_}2472{_}"Look here!"
10009{_}2473{_}said I; "gentlemen, this game is mine.
10009{_}2474{_}I claim him by right of discovery, and my right must not be interfered with."
10009{_}2475{_}"Very well," the Doctor answered, "we'll only take a hand in his capture if he's likely to escape.
10009{_}2476{_}So, go ahead."
10009{_}2477{_}As we came within a few yards of the shore, and we could see that the animal's hoofs touched the bottom, I aimed carefully at his head, and fired.
10009{_}2478{_}He made one desperate lunge forward, and turned over on his side, dying with scarcely a straggle, the ball having passed directly through his brain.
10009{_}2479{_}This was the first and only live moose I have ever seen.
10009{_}2480{_}He was not a large one, being, probably, a three-year-old, but well-grown.
10009{_}2481{_}We should have called him a monster, had we not, before that time, seen in various museums the stuffed skins of those a quarter or a third larger.
10009{_}2482{_}He would have weighed, as shot, probably between five and six hundred pounds.
10009{_}2483{_}He had made this solitary island his home, as we ascertained by his spoor and other signs that we found upon subsequent explorations.
10009{_}2484{_}We saw his bed but a few rods from where we landed, and from which our dogs had aroused him, though they, in their excitement, had overrun his scent, and dashed off after a deer.
10009{_}2485{_}We had now accomplished one of the objects of our journey in this direction, and as the law we had imposed upon ourselves had reached its limits, prohibiting our shooting another moose that day, even should an opportunity occur, we concluded to return to our shanty, on the lake below.
10009{_}2486{_}We, therefore, dressed our moose, and taking with us the skin and hind quarters, started down stream to a late dinner on Little Tupper's Lake.
10009{_}2487{_}Indeed, there was a sort of necessity for our doing so.
10009{_}2488{_}We had left our provisions there, calculating to return in the afternoon, not having taken with us even pepper or salt, wherewith to season the food which, upon constraint, we might cook during our absence.
10009{_}2489{_}A few crackers, in the pockets of each, was all, in the provision line, that we had provided ourselves with, and though, when we saw the moose-tracks in the sand, we had concluded to rough it, for a single night, for the chance of securing such rare game, yet having secured it, that part of our mission was accomplished, and we turned towards home.
10009{_}2490{_}On our return to the lake, Spalding and myself rowed across to the mouth of a cold brook, to procure a supply of fresh trout, upon which, with our moose and bear-meat, to dine.
10009{_}2491{_}This we soon accomplished, and on our arrival home, we found huge pieces of moose and bear roasting before a blazing fire.
10009{_}2492{_}The meat was supported upon long sticks, one end of which was sharpened, and the meat spitted upon it, and the other thrust into the ground, in a slanting direction, so as to bring the roasting pieces into a proper position before the fire.
10009{_}2493{_}The meat was removed occasionally, and turned, until the roasting process was completed, and then served up on clean birch bark, just peeled from the trees, in the place of platters.
10009{_}2494{_}We had tin plates, knives, and forks, with us, also a tea-kettle, tin cups, and tea of the choicest quality, sugar, pepper, salt, and pork.
10009{_}2495{_}The man who cannot make a meal where the viands present are moose-meat, bear, jerked venison, fresh trout, and pork, and for drink the best of tea and the purest and coldest spring water, had better keep out of the Rackett woods.
10009{_}2496{_}The people, whoever they were, who prepared the camp in which we were domiciled, had an eye to convenience and comfort.
10009{_}2497{_}The shanty was built of logs, on three sides, the crevices between which were filled with moss, and the sloping roof neatly covered with bark, in layers, like an old-fashioned roof, covered with split shingles.
10009{_}2498{_}The front was open, and directly before it was a rough fire-place, with jams, made of small boulders, laid up with clay, regularly-fashioned, as if intended for a kitchen.
10009{_}2499{_}This fire-place was three or four feet high, and served an excellent purpose, with reference to our cookery, and the lighting of our shanty at night.
10009{_}2500{_}It served, also, to conduct the smoke upward, and prevented it from being blown into our faces, as we sat in front, at once, of our sleeping-place and our camp-fire.
10009{_}2501{_}The only things that reminded us of civilization, aside from what we carried with us, were the innumerable crickets that, through all the night, kept up their chirruping in the crevices of this rude fireplace.
10009{_}2502{_}There was something old-fashioned and sociable in their song.
10009{_}2503{_}These, with the shrill notes of the little peepers along the shore, were old sounds to us, familiar voices, and they fell pleasantly on the ear.
10009{_}2504{_}We had finished our meal, and taken to our pipes in the evening, as the sun went down among the old forests, away off in the west.
10009{_}2505{_}The greyness of twilight came stealing over the water, and grew into darkness in the beautiful valley where that lake lay sleeping.
10009{_}2506{_}The stars stole out silently, and set their watch in the sky, and calmness and repose rested upon everything around us.
10009{_}2507{_}"I remember," said Smith, "the first year that I was in college, of hearing two learned professors disputing about what sort of animal it was that made the piping noise we hear in the marshy places, and stagnant pools, in the spring time, usually known as peepers.
10009{_}2508{_}One insisted that it was a newt, or small lizard; and I remember that he went to his library, and brought a volume which proved his theory to be correct.
10009{_}2509{_}The other denied the authority of the author, and insisted that the peeper was a frog.
10009{_}2510{_}The discussion excited my curiosity, and I made up my mind to satisfy myself on the subject, if possible, by occular demonstration.
10009{_}2511{_}There was a small marshy place, half a mile, or so, from the college grounds, from which I had heard, in my walks, the music of the peepers coming up every evening, in a loud and joyous chorus.
10009{_}2512{_}I watched by it a number of evenings, and though there were a plenty of peepers, piping merrily enough, yet I could not get sight of one to save me.
10009{_}2513{_}I began to think it was a myth, the viewless spirit of the bog, that made all the noises about which the learned professors had been disputing.
10009{_}2514{_}At last, however, I got sight of a peeper, caught him in the act, and saw that it was, in fact, a little frog, nothing more, nothing less.
10009{_}2515{_}He was not more than three feet from me, and though, when I moved, he hid himself in the muddy water, yet I managed to capture and take him home alive.
10009{_}2516{_}He was a little animal, certainly, not larger than a half-dollar piece, and it was marvellous how a thing so small could make such a loud and piercing noise.
10009{_}2517{_}I took him to my room, and placed him in a water-tight box, in which I fashioned an artificial bog, in the hope that he would confirm my testimony by his piping.
10009{_}2518{_}The second evening, as I sat in my room, poring over the recitations of the morrow, he lifted up his voice, loud, shrill, and clear, as when singing in his native marsh.
10009{_}2519{_}I hurried, in triumph, to the learned disputants about his identity, and in their presence, he furnished unanswerable evidence that the peeper was a frog, and not a newt.
10009{_}2520{_}I was complimented by both the learned pundits, as though I had added a great item to the aggregate of human knowledge."
10009{_}2521{_}"You _did_ do a great thing, my friend," said Spalding, "you solved a mystery about which men, wise in the learning of the books, had perhaps been disputing for centuries.
10009{_}2522{_}What are the peepers?
10009{_}2523{_}asked the naturalist, who listened to their piping notes from the marshy places in the spring time.
10009{_}2524{_}It was a matter of small practical importance, what they were.
10009{_}2525{_}Still it was a question which MIND wanted to have solved.
10009{_}2526{_}Its solution would do no great amount of good to the world.
10009{_}2527{_}But then it was a mystery which it was the business of mind to lay bare; and what more has science done in tracing the history and progress of this earth of ours, as written upon the rocks, among which geology has been so long delving?
10009{_}2528{_}'What are the peepers?'
10009{_}2529{_}asked the naturalist.
10009{_}2530{_}'They are newts, little lizards,' answers a learned pandit.
10009{_}2531{_}'They are spirits of the bog, myths, that hold their carnival in the early grass of the marshy pools,' says the theorist and poet, who _believes_ in the idealities of a poetic fancy.
10009{_}2532{_}'They are frogs,' says a third, who is ready to chop any amount of logic in favor of his system of frogology, and hereupon columns of argument, and pages of learned discussion, have been held over the identity of the jolly peepers of the spring-time.
10009{_}2533{_}"But you discarded logic, threw away argument, and came down to the sure demonstrations of sober fact.
10009{_}2534{_}You watched by the marshy pool, and caught the 'peeper' in the act, took him '_in flagrante, delicto_,' as the lawyers say, and thus ended the theoretical discussion about the 'peepers.'
10009{_}2535{_}You placed another fixed fact upon the page of natural history.
10009{_}2536{_}"And how often has the wisdom of the schools, the philosophy of the profoundest theorists, been overthrown by the simple demonstrations of practical facts?
10009{_}2537{_}For a thousand years the world was in pursuit of the giant power that lay hidden in heated vapor, the steam that came floating up from boiling water.
10009{_}2538{_}That power eluded the grasp and baffled the research of human genius, which was looking so earnestly after it, until ingenuity gave it up, and philosophy pronounced it a delusion.
10009{_}2539{_}Not far from the beginning of the present century, practical experiment began to develop the mysterious power of steam.
10009{_}2540{_}Rudely and imperfectly harnessed, at first, it still made the great wheel revolve, and men talked about making it a great motor for mechanical purposes.
10009{_}2541{_}Philosophy volunteered its demonstrations of the absolute impossibility of such a thing.
10009{_}2542{_}Still human ingenuity felt its way carefully onward, until the great fact was developed, that steam was in truth capable of moving machinery, was endowed almost with vitality, and could be made to throw the shuttle and spin.
10009{_}2543{_}Ingenious men hinted that it might be made to propel water-craft in the place of wind and sails, and thus be harnessed into the service of commerce, as it had already been into that of manufactures.
10009{_}2544{_}Here again philosophy interposed its axioms, and declared the scheme among the wild vagaries of a distempered fancy.
10009{_}2545{_}But years rolled on, and the tall ship that swung out upon the broad ocean, and moved forward when the air was still and calmness was on the face of the deep, forward in the eye of the wind--forward in the teeth of the storm, that stopped not for billow or blast, gave the lie to philosophy, and scattered the theory of the wise like chaff.
10009{_}2546{_}"The lightning, that fierce spirit of the storm, that darted down on its mission of destruction from the black cloud floating in the sky, became a thing of interest to the mechanical world, and the question was asked, 'Why cannot the lightning be harnessed into the service of man, and be made utilitarian?'
10009{_}2547{_}Philosophy sneered at the wild delusion, but see how that same subtle and mysterious agency has been conquered?
10009{_}2548{_}Note how truthfully it carries every word intrusted to its charge, along thousands of miles of the telegraph wire, with a speed, in comparison with which, sound is a laggard, a speed that annihilates alike space and time.
10009{_}2549{_}Men looked into a mirror, and seeing their own counterpart, a _fac-simile_ of themselves reflected there, began to ask, 'Why may not that shadow be fixed; fastened in some way, to remain upon the polished surface that gives it back, even after the original may be mouldering in the grave?'
10009{_}2550{_}Here again philosophy laid its finger upon its nose, and winked facetiously, as if it had found a new subject for ridicule, in the stupendous folly of such an inquiry.
10009{_}2551{_}But from that simple question, rose up the Daguerreian art; an art which fixes upon metallic plates, upon paper, the shadow of a man, of palace and cottage, of mountain and field, giving thus a picture ten thousand times truer to nature than the pencil of the cunningest artist.
10009{_}2552{_}These and a thousand other mighty triumphs of human ingenuity have fought their way onward to their present position, against the fogyism of philosophy, the inertia of the schoolmen.
10009{_}2553{_}They have been the sequence of cold, resistless demonstrations of experiment and fact.
10009{_}2554{_}The world would stand still but for the spirit of research for the practical; for experimental, and not theoretical knowledge, that is abroad.
10009{_}2555{_}It is this spirit that moves the world in all its present matchless career of progress, and distinguishes our era above all others of the world's existence.
10009{_}2556{_}You may be thankful, my friend, that you have been able to add another fixed fact to the stock of human knowledge, even though it be only that the 'peeper' is a frog, and not a 'newt' or a 'myth.'
10009{_}2557{_}"But who would suppose that such a tiny little frogling could make such a loud, shrill, and ear-piercing sound?
10009{_}2558{_}Who would think that a million of such puny things, could make the air of a summer evening so full of the music of their songs?
10009{_}2559{_}I remember how, in my boyhood, I listened to their voices, which came up loudest, shrillest, merriest, when twilight was spreading its grey mantle over the earth; while the song of the birds was hushing into silence, and the coming darkness was lulling the things of the day into repose; Oh!
10009{_}2560{_}how merrily they sang along the little brooklet that took its rise in a spring in the meadow, and wended its way among the young grass, just springing into verdure, to the beautiful lake beyond.
10009{_}2561{_}Their song is in my ear now, and that meadow, that beautiful lake, the tall hills on the summits of which the departing sunlight lingered, the tall maples that clustered in their conelike beauty around that gushing fountain, the clustered plum trees, the giant oak, spared by the woodman's axe when the old forest was swept away, the fields, the 'Gulf' in the hill-side, and the beautiful creek, that came cascading down the shelving rocks, and leaping over precipices in which the speckled trout sported: all these are before me now--a vision of loveliness, all the more dear because stamped upon the memory when life was young.
10009{_}2562{_}Oh!
10009{_}2563{_}Time!
10009{_}2564{_}Time!
10009{_}2565{_}the wrecks that lie scattered in thy pathway!
10009{_}2566{_}That little brooklet, and the peepers, the fountain, the maples, and the meadow, are all gone.
10009{_}2567{_}The brave old oak was riven by the lightning.
10009{_}2568{_}The fields have crept up to the very summit of the hills, and even the stream that came down from the mountain has vanished away, save when the rains, or the melting snows send it in a freshet over the rocks where, when I was a boy, it was cascading always.
10009{_}2569{_}That beautiful meadow, too, is gone, and the streets of a modern village, with blocks of houses, and stores, and shops, occupy the place where I swung my first scythe.
10009{_}2570{_}The old log-house vanished years and years ago.
10009{_}2571{_}A steamboat ploughs its way through that beautiful lake, and the things of my boyhood are but visions of memory, called up from the long, long past.
10009{_}2572{_}Not one landmark of the olden time remains.
10009{_}2573{_}Oh!
10009{_}2574{_}Time!
10009{_}2575{_}Time!"
10009{_}2576{_}CHAPTER XXIII.
10009{_}2577{_}THE CRICKET IN THE WALL--THE MINISTER'S ILLUSTRATION--OLD MEMORIES.
10009{_}2578{_}We spent the following day in drifting quietly around the lake, floating lazily in the little bays, under the shadow of the tall trees, and lounging upon small islands, gathering the low-bush whortleberries which grew in abundance upon them.
10009{_}2579{_}We filled our tin pails with this delicious fruit for a dessert for our evening meal.
10009{_}2580{_}On one of these islands we found indications of its being inhabited by wood rabbits, and we sent Cullen to the shanty for the dogs to course them, not however with any intention of capturing them, but to enjoy the music of the chase, and hear the voices of the hounds echoing over the water.
10009{_}2581{_}We landed them upon the island, and began beating for the game.
10009{_}2582{_}The hounds understanding that their business was the pursuit of deer, and having hunted the island over thoroughly, came back to us, and sat quietly down upon their haunches, as much as to say there was nothing there worth looking after.
10009{_}2583{_}But we had seen one of the little animals that had been roused from its bed by the dogs, and we called their special attention to the fact by leading them to the spot, and bidding them to "hunt him up."
10009{_}2584{_}They understood our meaning, and started on the trail, with a loud and cheerful cry.
10009{_}2585{_}For half an hour, they coursed him round and round the island, making the lake vocal with their merry music.
10009{_}2586{_}We might have shot the game they were pursuing fifty times, but we had no design against its life.
10009{_}2587{_}The little fellow did not seem to be greatly alarmed, for we noted him often, when by doubling he had temporarily thrown off the dogs, squat himself down, and throw his long ears back in the direction of the sound that had been pursuing him; and when the dogs straightened upon his trail, and approached where he sat, he would bound nimbly away among the thick bushes to double on them again.
10009{_}2588{_}We called off the dogs and passed on to float along under the shadow of the forest trees and the hills, and take an occasional trout by way of experiment among the broken rocks along the shore.
10009{_}2589{_}We had dispatched Cullen to the shanty to prepare dinner for us by six o'clock, at which hour we were to be at home.
10009{_}2590{_}Cullen had promised, to use his own expression, "to spread himself" in the preparation of this meal, and he kept his promise.
10009{_}2591{_}On our return, we found a sirloin of moose roasted to a turn, a stake of bear-meat broiled on the coals, a stew of jerked venison, and as pleasant a dish of fried trout and pork as an epicure could desire.
10009{_}2592{_}Our appetites were keen, and we did ample justice to his cookery.
10009{_}2593{_}This was one of the most delightful evenings that I have ever spent in the northern woods.
10009{_}2594{_}There was such a calm resting upon all things, such an impress of repose upon forest and lake, such a cheerful quiet and serenity all around us, that one could scarcely refrain from rejoicing aloud in the beauty and the glory of the hour.
10009{_}2595{_}As the sun sank to his rest behind the western hills, and the twilight began to gather in the forest and over the lake, the moon rose over the eastern high lands, walking with a queenly step up into the sky, casting a long line of brilliant light across the waters, showing the shadows of the mountains in bold outline in the depths below, and paling the stars by her brightness above.
10009{_}2596{_}We all felt that we were recruiting in strength so rapidly in these mountain regions, where the air was so bracing and pure, under the influence of exercise, simple diet, natural sleep, and the absence of the labors and cares of business, that we were contented, notwithstanding the monotony that began to mark our everyday proceedings.
