10009{_}3054{_}CHAPTER XXIX.
10009{_}3055{_}A MYSTERIOUS SOUND--TREED BY A MOOSE--ANGLING FOR A POWDER HORN--AN UNHEEDED WARNING AND THE CONSEQUENCES.
10009{_}3056{_}As Spalding ceased speaking, there came from away off, over the forest in the direction of the tall mountain peaks, a faint sound like the boom of a cannon, so distant that it could scarcely be heard, and yet it was distinct and palpable to the senses.
10009{_}3057{_}I say that it came from the direction of the mountains, seen dim and shadowy in the distance, and yet none of us were quite sure of this.
10009{_}3058{_}We all heard it, but not one of us could assert that the direction from which it came was a fixed fact in his mind.
10009{_}3059{_}"There, Judge" said Cullen, "I've hearn that sound often among the mountains, and when I've been driftin' about on these lakes, it never seems much louder or nearer.
10009{_}3060{_}It always seems to come from the mountains, and yet you'll hear it while shantyin' at their base, and it sounds just as faint and far off as it did just now.
10009{_}3061{_}What it is, or where it comes from, I won't undertake to say.
10009{_}3062{_}The old Ingins who, five and twenty year ago, fished and hunted over these regions, told of it as a thing to wonder at, and that it was handed along down from generation to generation, as one of the mysteries of this wilderness.
10009{_}3063{_}I mind once I was out among the Adirondacks, trappin' martin and sable.
10009{_}3064{_}I shantied for a week with Crop, under the shadow of Mount Marcy.
10009{_}3065{_}It was twenty odd year ago, and that old mountain stood a good deal further from a clearin' than it does now.
10009{_}3066{_}Crop and I had a good many hard days' work that trip; but we got a full pack of martin and sable skins, and two or three wolf scalps, besides a bear and a painter, and we didn't complain.
10009{_}3067{_}Wal, one afternoon, we put up a shanty in an open spot two miles from our regular campin' ground, and built our fire for the night.
10009{_}3068{_}There was no moon, and though the stars shone out bright and clear, yet in the deep shadow of the forest it was dark and gloomy enough.
10009{_}3069{_}We had eaten our supper, and I was smokin' my last pipe before layin' myself away, when all at once the forest was lighted up like the day.
10009{_}3070{_}It was all the more light from the sudden glare which broke upon the darkness, and there, for an instant, stood the old woods, lighted up like noon, every tree distinct, every mountain, every rock, and valley, as perfect and plain to be seen as if the sun was standin' right above us in the sky.
10009{_}3071{_}Crop was as much astonished as I was, and he crept to my feet and trembled like a coward, as he crouched beside them.
10009{_}3072{_}I looked up, and flyin' across the heavens was a great ball of fire, lookin' for all the world as if the sun had broke loose, and was runnin' away in a fright.
10009{_}3073{_}A long trail of light flashed and streamed along the sky where it passed.
10009{_}3074{_}It was out of sight in a moment, and the fiery tail it left behind faded into darkness.
10009{_}3075{_}A little while after, maybe ten minutes after it disappeared, that boomin' sound came driftin' down the wind, and I somehow tho't it was mixed up in some way with that great ball of fire that flew across the sky.
10009{_}3076{_}Maybe I was wrong, but I've always tho't it was the bustin' into pieces of that fiery thing that lighted up the old woods that night, that broke the forest stillness, like a far off cannon.
10009{_}3077{_}I never heard it so loud at any other time, and when I hear it now, I always say to myself, there goes another of Nater's fireballs into shivers.
10009{_}3078{_}I've hearn it in the daytime, when the air was still, and the forest voices were hushed, but I never at any other time, day or night, saw what I suspicioned occasioned it.
10009{_}3079{_}The Ingins used to say it came from the mountains, but it don't.
10009{_}3080{_}I've hearn some folks pretend that it comes from the bowels of the airth, but it don't; its a thing of the air, and I've a notion it travels a mighty long way from its startin' place afore it reaches us.
10009{_}3081{_}"Talkin' about that trip among the Adirondacks, puts me in mind of an adventer I had with a bull moose, on one occasion among them.
10009{_}3082{_}There are times when sich an animal is dangerous.
10009{_}3083{_}I've hearn tell of elephants gittin' crazy and breakin' loose from their keepers, or killin' them, and makin' a general smash of whatever comes in their way.
10009{_}3084{_}I believe its so sometimes with a bull moose; and when the fit is on the animal forgets its timid nater, and is bold and fierce as a tiger.
10009{_}3085{_}I've seen two sich in my day; one of 'em sent me into a tree, and the other put me around a great hemlock a dozen or twenty times, a good deal faster than I like to travel in a general way, and if I hadn't hamstrung him with my huntin' knife, maybe he'd have been chasin' me round that tree yet.
10009{_}3086{_}Wal, as I was sayin' I was out among the Adirondacks one fall, airly in November; I'd wounded a deer, and sent Crop forward on his trail to overtake and secure him.
10009{_}3087{_}It was a big buck, with long horns, and Crop had a pretty good general idea of what sich things meant.
10009{_}3088{_}He was cautious about cultivatin' too close an acquaintance with such an animal, unless something oncommon obligated him to do so.
10009{_}3089{_}I heard him bayin' a little way over a ridge layin' gist beyond where I shot the buck.
10009{_}3090{_}I warn't in any great hurry, for I knew Crop would attend to his case, and I tho't I'd wipe out my rifle afore I loaded it again.
10009{_}3091{_}I was standin' by the upturned roots of a tall fir tree that had been blown down, and in fallin' had lodged in a crotch of a great birch, maybe twenty feet from the ground, and broke off.
10009{_}3092{_}I stepped onto the butt of the fallen spruce, and was takin' my time to clean my gun, when I heard a crashin' among the brush on the other side of the ridge, as if some mighty big animal was comin' my way.
10009{_}3093{_}I walked pretty quick along up the slopin' log till I was, maybe fifteen feet from the ground, and I saw Crop comin' over the ridge, in what the Doctor would call a high state of narvous excitement, with his tail between his legs, lookin' back over his shoulder, and expressin' his astonishment in a low, quick bark, at every jump, at something he seemed to regard as mighty onpleasant on his trail.
10009{_}3094{_}I didn't have to wait long to find out what it was, for about the biggest bull moose I ever happened to see, came crashin' like a steam-engine after him.
10009{_}3095{_}He wasn't more than two rods behind the dog, and if I ever saw an ugly looking beast, that moose was the one.
10009{_}3096{_}Every hair seemed to stand towards his head, and if he wasn't in earnest I never saw an animal that was.
10009{_}3097{_}He was puttin' in his best jumps, and the way he hurried up Crop's cakes was a thing to be astonished at.
10009{_}3098{_}The dog didn't see me, and seemed to be principled agin stoppin' to inquire my whereabouts.
10009{_}3099{_}He dashed under the log where I stood, and the moose after him like mad.
10009{_}3100{_}He seemed to be expectin' aid and comfort from me, as the papers say, and was wonderin', no doubt, where me and my rifle was all this time.
10009{_}3101{_}I called after him, but he was in a hurry and couldn't stop, for there was a thing he didn't care about shakin' hands with, not three rods from his tail.
10009{_}3102{_}He heard me, though, and took a circle round a great boulder, and the moose after him, and as he got straightened my way, I called him again, and he saw me.
10009{_}3103{_}He leaped onto the log and came runnin' up to where I stood, and was mighty glad to be out of the way of them big hoofs and horns that were after him.
10009{_}3104{_}He was safe now, and he opened his mouth and let off a good deal of tall barkin' at his enemy.
10009{_}3105{_}The moose saw us, and his fury was the greater because he couldn't get at us.
10009{_}3106{_}He kept chargin' back and forth under the log we were perched on, and if there wasn't malice in his eye, I wouldn't say so.
10009{_}3107{_}"When I first saw him, I was standin' with the butt of my rifle on the log, my hand graspin' the barrel, and as I caught it up suddenly to load, the string of my powder-horn caught between the muzzle and the ramrod, broke, and the horn fell to the ground.
10009{_}3108{_}Here was a fix for a hunter to be in.
10009{_}3109{_}My rifle was empty, and every grain of powder I had in the world was in the horn, fifteen feet below me, on the ground.
10009{_}3110{_}To go down after it was a thing I was principled agin undertaking considerin' the circumstance of that bull moose with his great horns and the onpleasant temper he seemed to be in.
10009{_}3111{_}What to do I didn't know.
10009{_}3112{_}I hollered and shouted at the kritter, thinkin', maybe, that the voice of a human might scare him; but it only made him madder, and every time I hollered he charged under the log more furiously than before.
10009{_}3113{_}I threw my huntin' cap at him, but he pitched into it, and if he didn't trample it into the ground, as if it was a human, you may shoot me.
10009{_}3114{_}After a while, he got tired of dashin' back and forth, under the log, and took a stand two or three rods off, and as he eyed us, shook his great horns and stamped with his big hoofs, as much as to say, 'very well, gentlemen, I can wait, don't hurry yourselves, take your time; but I shall stay here as long as you stay up there.
10009{_}3115{_}And when you do come down, we'll take a turn that won't be pleasant to some of us.'
10009{_}3116{_}Crop and I took the hint and sat still, thinkin' maybe he'd get over his pet and move off; but he did'nt lean that way at all.
10009{_}3117{_}He seemed to've made up his mind to stay there as long as we stayed on the log, be the same more or less.
10009{_}3118{_}We'd sat there maybe an hour, when I happened to think of a trollin' line and some fishhooks I had in my pocket, and it came across me that possibly I might fish up my powder horn.
10009{_}3119{_}So tyin' half a dozen hooks to the end of my line, I laid down on the log to angle for my powder-horn.
10009{_}3120{_}When I laid down, the old bull made a pass under the log, as if he expected me down there, and charged back again, as if he was disappointed in not runnin' agin me.
10009{_}3121{_}But he saw 'twan't no use, and took his old stand agin.
10009{_}3122{_}I dropped down the grapnel, and after a great many failures, I hooked into the string of the powder horn, and hoisted away.
10009{_}3123{_}I hauled it up mighty quick, for the old bull seemed to be suspicions that something was goin' on that might have something to do with his futer happiness, and when he got sight of it, the pass he made was a thing to stand out of the way of.
10009{_}3124{_}But he was too late; the powder-horn was safe, and I notified him, as Squire Smith did the cats, to leave them parts in just one minute by the clock.
10009{_}3125{_}He did'nt pay any attention to the warnin'.
10009{_}3126{_}I loaded my rifle carefully, and while I was puttin' on the cap, asked the gentleman if he calculated to move on, and let peaceable people alone.
10009{_}3127{_}He didn't condescend to answer a word, looking for all the world like a tiger in savageness.
10009{_}3128{_}'Very well,' said I, as I sighted him between the eyes, 'on your head be it,' and pulled.
10009{_}3129{_}The ball went crashin' through his skull into his brain, and he went down.
10009{_}3130{_}Crop knew what that meant.
10009{_}3131{_}He didn't wait to run down the log, but leaped to the ground, and had his teeth in the animal's throat before the echoes of my rifle were done dancin' around among the mountains.
10009{_}3132{_}I loaded my gun before I came down, thinkin' maybe there might be another bad tempered moose about, but there wasn't.
10009{_}3133{_}Crop and I learned what we ought to've know before, and that was that it's a safe thing for a hunter to have an extra horn of powder in his pocket, and a loaded rifle in his hand when a mad bull moose is on his trail, and that a slantin' tree is a good thing to get onto at sich a time."
10009{_}3134{_}CHAPTER XXX.
10009{_}3135{_}GOOD-BYE--FLOATING DOWN THE RACKETT--A BLACK FOX--A TRICK UPON THE MARTIN TRAPPERS AND ITS CONSEQUENCES.
10009{_}3136{_}We rose with the dawn the next morning, and before the sun was above the hills we were on our way down the lake, to separate as we struck the Rackett; the Doctor and Smith to return by the way of Keeseville and the Champlain, and Spalding and myself to drift down that pleasant stream to Pottsdam, and thence to the majestic St. Lawrence, to spend a fortnight among the "Thousand Islands" of that noble river.
10009{_}3137{_}Near the outlet of the lake is a bold rocky bluff, rising right up out of the deep water twenty feet, against which the waves dash, and around which a romantic bay steals away to hide itself in the old woods.
10009{_}3138{_}This beautiful bay is always calm, for even the narrow strait which connects it with the open water is divided by a rocky, but wooded island, shutting out alike the winds and the waves from disturbing its repose.
10009{_}3139{_}It is surrounded by gigantic forest trees, whose shadows make it a cool retreat in the heat of noon, and whose dense foliage fills the air with freshness and fragrance when the sun is hot in the sky.
10009{_}3140{_}Towards its head, a cold stream comes creeping around the boulders, and dancing and singing down the rocks from a copious spring, a short way back in the forest.
10009{_}3141{_}Near where this brook enters we landed at seven o'clock to breakfast.
10009{_}3142{_}We supplied ourselves with fish by casting across the mouth of the little stream, while our boatmen were preparing a fire.
10009{_}3143{_}Our sail of eight miles down the lake furnished us with appetites which gave to the beautiful speckled trout we caught there a peculiar relish.
10009{_}3144{_}We arranged matters so that the Doctor and Smith were to return in one boat to the Saranacs, while Spalding and myself were to move on down the Rackett with the other two.
10009{_}3145{_}Cullen and Wood were to go with us to Pottsdam, from whence our route lay by railroad to Ogdensburgh.
10009{_}3146{_}We had, on entering the woods, dispatched our baggage to the former place to await our arrival there.
10009{_}3147{_}At nine o'clock we launched out upon the lake again.
10009{_}3148{_}There are two outlets which enter the Rackett, half a mile apart, down the right hand one of which the Doctor and Smith's course lay, and ours down the left.
10009{_}3149{_}We shook hands with our friends, and lay upon our oars while they passed on towards home, wishing them a pleasant voyage, and a safe return.
10009{_}3150{_}"I say," shouted Smith, as they were about rounding a point that would hide them from our view, "remember our compact about killing the bear.
10009{_}3151{_}The glory of that achievement belongs to me, you know.
10009{_}3152{_}Don't say a word about it when you get home till you see me.
10009{_}3153{_}I haven't fully made up my mind as to the manner of capturing him, and there must be no contradictions on the subject."
10009{_}3154{_}"Go ahead," replied Spalding, "we'll be careful of your honor.
10009{_}3155{_}Drop us a line at Cape Vincent, when you've digested the matter, and we'll stand by you.
10009{_}3156{_}Good-bye!"
10009{_}3157{_}"Good-bye!"
10009{_}3158{_}And our friends disappeared from our sight on their voyage home.
10009{_}3159{_}"And so," said Spalding, "we are to leave this beautiful lake, and these old forests so soon.
10009{_}3160{_}I could linger here a month still, enjoying these shady and primitive solitudes.
10009{_}3161{_}To you and I, the quiet which one finds here is vastly more inviting than it is to the friends who have just left us.
10009{_}3162{_}The Doctor, of necessity, leads a life of activity, feeling physical weariness as the result of his labors, but little of that strong yearning for intellectual repose which those in your profession or mine so often feel.
10009{_}3163{_}Smith's life demands excitement.
10009{_}3164{_}The absence of the cares and toil of business occasions a restlessness and desire of change, which makes him discontented here.
10009{_}3165{_}With them the great charm of this wild region is its novelty.
10009{_}3166{_}They enjoy its beauties for a season with peculiar relish, but as these become familiar, the spell is broken, and they turn towards home without a regret To you and I, there is something beyond this.
10009{_}3167{_}We, too, feel and appreciate the beauty of these lakes and mountains The hill-sides and placid waters, the forest songs, and wild scenery are pleasant to us; but we enjoy them the more from the intellectual relaxation, the mental quiet and repose, which we find among them.
10009{_}3168{_}We feel that we are resting, that the process of recuperation, intellectual as well as physical, is going on within us.
10009{_}3169{_}We can almost trace its progress, and we feel that the time spent by us here is full of profit as well as pleasure.
10009{_}3170{_}At all events, it is so with me, and if duty to others, whose interests it is my business to serve, did not demand my return, I could enjoy another month here with unabated pleasure."
10009{_}3171{_}"You have left me little," I replied, "to add to what you have already said, in expressing the sources of my enjoyment among these beautiful lakes.
10009{_}3172{_}Fishing and hunting, considered in the abstract, are things I care but little about.
10009{_}3173{_}They are pleasant enough in their way, but what brings me here is the strong desire as well as necessity for the repose of which you speak.
10009{_}3174{_}There is a luxury in intellectual rest, when the brain is wearied with protracted toil, which far surpasses the mere animal enjoyment which follows relaxation from physical labor.
10009{_}3175{_}That rest I cannot find in society.
10009{_}3176{_}I must seek it among wild and primeval solitudes, where I can be alone with nature in her unadorned simplicity, away from the barbarisms, so to speak, of civilization, where I can act and talk and think as a natural, and not an artificial man, where I can be off my guard, and free from the weight of that armor which the conventionalities of life, the captions espionage of the world compels us to wear, un-tempted by the thousand enticements which society everywhere presents to lure us into unrest."
10009{_}3177{_}We drifted leisurely down the left hand channel, and entered the Rackett, bidding good-bye to the beautiful lake as a bend in the river hid it from our view.
10009{_}3178{_}A mile below the junction, the river runs square against a precipice some sixty feet in height, wheeling off at a right angle, and stretching away though a natural meadow on either hand, of hundreds of acres in extent.
10009{_}3179{_}At the base of this precipice, formed by the rocky point of a hill, the water is of unknown depth.
10009{_}3180{_}Above, and fifty feet from the surface of the river, there are ledges of a foot or two in width, like shelves, along which the fox, the fisher, and possibly the panther, creep, instead of travelling over the high ridge extending back into the forest.
10009{_}3181{_}As we rounded a point which brought us in view of this precipice, Spalding, who was in the forward boat, discovered a black object making its way along the face of the rocks.
10009{_}3182{_}A signal for silence was given, and the boats were permitted to float with the current in the direction of the precipice.
10009{_}3183{_}We were forty rods distant, and the animal, whatever it was, had no suspicion of danger.
10009{_}3184{_}It paused midway across the rocks, looked about, nosing out over the water, and sat down upon its haunches, as if enjoying the beauty of the scenery around it.
10009{_}3185{_}In the meantime, the boats had drifted within twenty rods, and Spalding, taking deliberate aim, fired.
10009{_}3186{_}At the crack of the rifle, the animal leapt dear of the ledge, struck once against the face of the rock some twenty feet below, and then went, end over end, thirty feet into the river.
10009{_}3187{_}As he struck the water he commenced swimming round and round in a circle, evidently bewildered by Spalding's bullet, or the effect of his involuntary plunge down the rocks.
10009{_}3188{_}Our men bent to their oars, and had got within five or six rods of it, when it straightened up in alarm for the shore.
10009{_}3189{_}"Hold on, Cullen," said I, "lay steady for a moment."
10009{_}3190{_}I drew upon the animal, and just as it reached the shore, fired, and it turned over dead.
10009{_}3191{_}We found it to be a black fox, that had walked out upon the ledge, and thus been added another victim to the indulgence of an idle curiosity.
10009{_}3192{_}Spalding's bullet had grazed its belly, raking off the hair and graining the skin; mine had gone through its head.
10009{_}3193{_}"There, Judge," said Cullen, as he lifted the animal into the boat, "is a kritter that isn't often met with in these parts, and the wonder is, that he didn't discover us as we floated down the stream.
10009{_}3194{_}He's about the cunningest animal that travels the woods.
10009{_}3195{_}He's got an eye that's always open, a delicate ear, and a sharp nose, and he keeps 'em busy, as a general thing.
10009{_}3196{_}He never neglects their warnin', but puts out about the quickest, whenever they notify him that there's an enemy about.
10009{_}3197{_}I've had a good deal of trouble with them in my day, when I've been out trappin' martin.
10009{_}3198{_}They'll manage to spring the trap and carry off the bait.
10009{_}3199{_}When one of them chaps gets on a line of traps, there's no use in talkin'.
10009{_}3200{_}The game's up, and the trapper may make up his mind to get rid of the varmint in some way, or locate in another range of country.
10009{_}3201{_}He'll find his traps sprung and his bait gone.
10009{_}3202{_}Or if a martin has been in ahead of the fox, he'll find only the skull, the end of the tail, the feet, and a few of the larger bones, and they'll be picked mighty clean at that.
10009{_}3203{_}You've seen a martin trap, or if you haven't, I'll try and describe one so that you'll understand it.
10009{_}3204{_}It's a very simple contrivance, and if a martin was not a good deal more stupid than a goose, he'd never be caught in one of them.
10009{_}3205{_}We drive down a couple of rows of little stakes, plantin' the stakes close together, and leaving between the rows a space of six or eight inches.
10009{_}3206{_}The rows are may be a foot and a half long.
10009{_}3207{_}We then cut and trim a long saplin', say five or six inches across at the butt, and leaving one end on the ground, set the other, may be two feet high, with a kind of figure four, so that when it falls, it will come down between the rows of stakes.
10009{_}3208{_}We fix the bait so that a martin in getting at it, will have to go in between the rows of stakes, and displace the trap sticks, when down comes the pole upon him and crushes him to death.
10009{_}3209{_}We talk about a _line_ of traps, because we blaze a line of trees, sometimes for miles, and set a trap every twenty or thirty rods.
10009{_}3210{_}I've had a line of a dozen miles or more, in my day, in a circle around my campin' ground.
10009{_}3211{_}In minding our traps, we follow the line of marked trees from one to the other, and so never miss a trap, nor get lost in the woods.
10009{_}3212{_}"I mind once, a good many years ago, Crop and I was over towards the St. Regis, on a cruise after martin and sable, and anything else in the way of game we could pick up.
10009{_}3213{_}I'd laid out my trappin' arrangements on a pretty large scale, and was doin' a little better than midlin', when I found that my traps were sprung by some animal that helped himself to the bait, without leavin' his hide as a consideration for settin' of 'em.
10009{_}3214{_}After a few days, I found that whatever it was, understood the line as well as I did, for he took the range regular, and not only stole the bait, but ate up half a dozen martin, that had given me a claim on their hides by springin' my traps.
10009{_}3215{_}This was a kind of medlin' with my private concerns that I didn't like, and I was bound to find out who the interloper was, and if possible, to make his acquaintance.
10009{_}3216{_}There was no snow on the ground, and I couldn't get at his track.
10009{_}3217{_}So I made up my mind to watch for him.
10009{_}3218{_}Well, one day I spoke to Crop to stay by the shanty and take care of the things, while I went to find out who it was that was medlin' with our property, and started off on my line of traps.
10009{_}3219{_}I got up into the crotch of a great birch near one of 'em, and sat there with my rifle, waitin' for something to turn up.
10009{_}3220{_}It was a little after noon when I got located.
10009{_}3221{_}The sun travelled slowly along down towards the western hills, his bright light, in that calm November day, makin' the rocky ranges and the bare heads of the tall peaks shine out in a blaze of glory.
10009{_}3222{_}The livin' things of the old woods were busy and jolly enough.
10009{_}3223{_}An old owl came flying lazily out of the thick branches of a hemlock, and lightin' within a dozen feet of me, opened his great round eyes in astonishment, and as the bright sunlight dazzled him, he squinted and turned his cat-like face from side to side, as if makin' up his mind that he'd know me the next time we met.
10009{_}3224{_}By-and-by he opened his hooked beak, and great red mouth, and roared out, 'Hoo!
10009{_}3225{_}hohoo!
10009{_}3226{_}hoo!'
10009{_}3227{_}as much as to say, 'who the devil are you?'
10009{_}3228{_}I didn't answer a word, and after a little, he flew back to his shadowy perch among the dense foliage of the hemlock.
10009{_}3229{_}A black squirrel came hopping along with his mouth full of beech nuts, and running nimbly up the tree on which I was perched, and out upon one of the great limbs, deposited his store in a hollow he found there.
10009{_}3230{_}He caught sight of me as he came back, and seating himself upon a branch, not six feet from my head, began chatterin' and barkin' as if givin' me a regular lecter for invadin' his premises, and takin' possession of his tree.
10009{_}3231{_}He didn't seem to understand the matter at all, and I didn't undertake to explain the reason of my being there.
10009{_}3232{_}After a little, he went off about his business, and left me to attend to mine.
10009{_}3233{_}A raccoon came nosing along, stoppin' every little way to turn over the leaves, or pull away the dirt from a root with his long hands, tastin' of one thing and smellin' of another in a mighty dainty way.
10009{_}3234{_}When he came to my tree, he seemed to think that there might be something among its branches worth looking at.
10009{_}3235{_}So he came clambering up its rough bark towards where I sat.
10009{_}3236{_}He came up on the other side of the tree from me, till he got about even with my huntin'-cap, and then came round to my side, and there we were, face to face, not two feet apart.
10009{_}3237{_}I reckon that coon was astonished when our eyes met, for with a sort of scream he let right loose, and dropped twenty feet to the ground like a clod, and the way he waddled away into the brash, mutterin' and talkin' to himself, was a thing to laugh at.
10009{_}3238{_}"The sun was, may be, an hour high, when lookin' along the line of marked trees, I saw a black animal come trotting mighty softly towards the trap I was watchin'.
10009{_}3239{_}I knew him at once.
10009{_}3240{_}He was a black fox, and I knew that he was the gentleman that had been makin' free with my property for the last few days.
10009{_}3241{_}He trotted up to the trap, and walked carefully around it, nosin' out towards the bait, but keepin' out from under the pole.
10009{_}3242{_}He seemed to understand what that pole meant, and that if it fell on him, he'd be very likely to be hurt.
10009{_}3243{_}After a little, he trotted out to the other end of the pole, and gettin' on to it, walked carefully along to within ten or twelve feet of the bait; if he didn't begin jumpin' up and down till he sprung the trap, you may shoot me.
10009{_}3244{_}When he'd done that job, he went back, and gettin' hold of the bait with his teeth, drew it out and began very cooly to eat it.
10009{_}3245{_}By this time I'd brought my rifle to bear upon the gentleman, but I gave him a little law, to see what his next move would be.
10009{_}3246{_}After he'd finished the bait, and found there warn't any more to be come at, he stretched himself on his belly along the ground, and began lickin' his paws, and passing them over his cheeks, as you've seen a cat do.
10009{_}3247{_}After he'd washed his face awhile, he sat himself down on his haunches, curled his long bushy tail around his feet, and looked about as if considerin' what he should do next.
10009{_}3248{_}Just then I paid my respects to him, and as my rifle broke the stillness of the forest, he turned a double summerset, and after kickin' around a little, laid still.
10009{_}3249{_}I came down from my perch, and took the gentleman to the shanty and added his hide to those of the martins I'd taken.
10009{_}3250{_}My traps warn't disturbed after that, and I carried home a pack of furs that bro't me near two hundred dollars."
10009{_}3251{_}CHAPTER XXXI.
10009{_}3252{_}OUT OF THE WOODS--THE THOUSAND ISLANDS--CAPE VINCENT--BASS FISHING HOME--A SEARCHER AFTER TRUTH--AN INTERRUPTION--FINIS.
10009{_}3253{_}We floated quietly down the Rackett, carrying our boats around the falls, shooting like an arrow down the rapids, or gliding along under the shadows of the gigantic forest trees that line the long, calm reaches of that beautiful river.
10009{_}3254{_}We shook hands and parted with our boatmen at the pleasant village of Pottsdam, where we arrived the second evening after leaving Tupper's Lake.
10009{_}3255{_}We found our baggage, and it was a pleasant thing to change our long beards for shaved faces, and our forest costume for the garniture of the outer man after the fashion of civilization.
10009{_}3256{_}We took the cars for Ogdensburgh, and the next morning found us steaming up the majestic St. Lawrence, towards that paradise of fishermen, the Thousand Islands.
10009{_}3257{_}We stopped a couple of days at Alexandria Bay, and passed on to Cape Vincent, a beautiful village situated a mile or two below where the river takes its departure from the broad lake beyond.
10009{_}3258{_}This pleasant little town is built upon a wide sweep of tableland, overlooking the river in front, and the open lake on the west.
10009{_}3259{_}It is accessible both by the lake and river, having two or three arrivals' and departures of steamboats each way daily, and being the terminus of the Rome and Watertown Railroad, the great thoroughfare between Kingston and the central portion of the Tipper Provinces and the States.
10009{_}3260{_}It is a delightful place in the hot summer months, with a climate unequalled for healthfulness, a cool breeze always fanning it from the water, and in the vicinity the best bass fishing to be found on this continent.
10009{_}3261{_}Opposite, and just below the town, is Carlton Island, on which stand the ruins of an old French fortification, the walls and trenches and the solitary chimneys, from which the wooden barracks have rotted or been burned away, remain as melancholy testimonials of the bloody strifes between the red men of the forest, and the pioneers of civilization who were driving them from the hunting grounds of their fathers.
10009{_}3262{_}The black bass of the St. Lawrence and Ontario, are the "gamest" fish that swim, and they are nowhere found in such abundance as in the neighborhood of Cape Vincent.
10009{_}3263{_}On the outer edge of the bar, near the head of Carlton Island, we caught between seventy and eighty in one afternoon, weighing from three to five pounds each, every one of which fought like a hero, diving with a plunge for the bottom, skiving with a rush down, across, or up the river; leaping clear from the water and shaking his head furiously, to throw the hook loose from his jaw, before surrendering to his fate.
10009{_}3264{_}In Wilson's Bay, a sweet place, three miles from the village by water, or one and a half by land, we caught as many more on another afternoon.
10009{_}3265{_}We took a sail-boat and glided round Lighthouse Point (a pleasant drive of two miles from the village), out into the lake, and steered for Grenadier Island, five miles distant, on which we tented for the night, and the bass we brought home the next day were something worth looking at.
10009{_}3266{_}Near the upper end of Long Island are other prolific bass shoals, where the fisherman may enjoy himself.
10009{_}3267{_}Indeed, he can scarcely go amiss in the surrounding waters.
10009{_}3268{_}The black bass of the St. Lawrence are not only game fish, but are, in excellence of flavor, scarcely excelled by any fish of this country.
10009{_}3269{_}Baked or boiled, they have few superiors, and as a pan fish, are excelled only by the brook-trout of the streams.
10009{_}3270{_}The season for taking them commences in July; and continues through September.
10009{_}3271{_}August is the best month in the year for the bass fishermen.
10009{_}3272{_}If, during that month, he will supply himself with a strong bass-pole, a strong treble-action reel, stout silk lines, and proper hooks, and visit Gape Vincent, he will find boatmen with a supply of minnows, ready to serve him; and if he fails to enjoy himself for a fortnight among the black bass of the St Lawrence and Ontario, he may count himself as a man who is very hard to please.
10009{_}3273{_}We spent a pleasant week at Cape Vincent, and then turned our faces homeward, invigorated in strength and buoyant in spirits, to begin again a round of toil, from which we, at least, could claim no further exemption.
10009{_}3274{_}"H----," said a friend of mine, as he stalked into my sanctum, a few days after my return, and seated himself at my elbow, as if for a private and confidential talk, "did Smith really shoot the bear, the skin of which he brought home, and which he exhibits with such triumph.
10009{_}3275{_}Tell me, honestly, as between you and me, did he in fact shoot him?"
10009{_}3276{_}"Smith certainly did shoot that bear," I replied.
10009{_}3277{_}"But is the marvellous story he tells about the manner of killing him really true?"
10009{_}3278{_}"That, of course, I cannot tell," I replied, "as I have never heard the story."
10009{_}3279{_}"Why," said my friend, "he tells about a beautiful lake, lying away back in the northern wilderness, above which Mount Marcy, and Mount Seward, and other nameless peaks of the Adirondacks, rear their tall heads to the clouds, throwing back the sunlight in a blaze of glory; on which the moonbeams lie like a mantle of silver, while away down in its fathomless depths the stars glow and sparkle, like the sheen of a million of diamonds.
10009{_}3280{_}Of the old forests and trees of fabulous growth, stretching away and away on every hand, throwing their sombre shadows far out over the water, in whose tangled recesses countless deer and moose, and panthers, and bears range, and among whose branches birds of unknown melody carol.
10009{_}3281{_}That one side of this beautiful lake is palisadoed by a wall of rocks, stand straight up sixty feet high, near the top of which is a shelf or narrow pathway, along which two men can scarcely walk abreast.
10009{_}3282{_}That he was passing along this pathway one afternoon, examining the rocks, and looking for geological specimens.
10009{_}3283{_}Below him was a precipice of fifty feet, against the base of which the waves, when the winds swept over the lake, dashed.
10009{_}3284{_}Around him the birds that build their nests in the crevices of the rock were whirling and screaming, while before him lay the beautiful lake, motionless and calm, as if it had fallen asleep and was slumbering sweetly in its forest bed.
10009{_}3285{_}That he was passing leisurely along with his rifle at a trail, admiring the transcendent loveliness of the scenery around him, where the rugged and the sublime, the placid and the beautiful, were so magnificently mingled, when, in turning a sharp angle, a huge bear"  "Copy!"
10009{_}3286{_}shouted the printer's devil, as he came plunging down three steps at a bound from the compositors' room above.
10009{_}3287{_}"Copy!"
10009{_}3288{_}he screamed, as he dove into the outer office where that article was usually kept, but found none.
10009{_}3289{_}"Mr. H.," said he, as he opened my door so gently, with a voice so quiet, and a look so innocent, that one might well be excused for believing that he had never spoken a loud word in his life, "Mr. H----, the foreman desired me to ask you for some copy."
10009{_}3290{_}"You see, my friend," said I to the anxious inquirer after truth, "that I am exceedingly busy just now.
10009{_}3291{_}You will excuse me, therefore, for referring you to the Doctor and Spalding, who know all about the matter.
10009{_}3292{_}Good day."
10009{_}3293{_}And my friend departed without finishing the story Smith told him about his killing the bear.
10009{_}3294{_}I have never heard the balance of that story yet.
10009{_}3295{_}And now, Reader, a word to you, and I have done.
10009{_}3296{_}When the sun comes up over the city, day after day, pouring his burning rays along the glimmering streets, shining on and on in a changeless glare, till he hides himself in the darkness again; when your strength wilts under the enervating influences of the summer heats, and you pant for the forest breezes and the "cooling streams," remember that the same wild region I have been describing, the same pleasant rivers, beautiful lakes, tall mountains, and primeval forests are there still, all inviting you to test their recuperative agencies.
10009{_}3297{_}The same singing birds, the fishes and the game are there waiting your pleasure.
10009{_}3298{_}Visit them when the summer heat makes the cities a desolation.
10009{_}3299{_}Give a month to the enjoyment of a wilderness-life, and you will return to your labors invigorated in strength, buoyant in spirit--a wiser, healthier, and a better man.
10009{_}3300{_}FINIS.
